Why Two Mutually Exclusive Disjunctives Yield No Conclusion
Author asserts that two mana'at al-jam' disjunctives yield no conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a pair of disjunctive premises yields a valid conclusion and identify the conditions under which they fail.

Core Idea
Two mana'at al-jam' disjunctives never produce a valid conclusion.

In Islamic logic, disjunctive syllogisms have strict conditions. When both premises are mana'at al-jam' (mutually exclusive disjunctions), the author shows they yield no valid result—neither a conjunctive nor a disjunctive conclusion. This lesson explores why and illustrates with examples from the source text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of each premise
Recognize that both premises are 'mana'at al-jam'' disjunctives—each excludes the conjunction of its disjuncts (i.e., they are mutually exclusive).
Example: 'Either X is a human or not an animal' and 'Either X is not an animal or a horse'.
2
Test for possible conclusions
Check whether a conjunctive (if-then) or a disjunctive (either-or) conclusion follows. The author states none does.
Any attempt to derive a conclusion fails regardless of arrangement.
3
Examine the given examples
The first example ('human or not animal' and 'not animal or horse') yields nothing. Changing the major to 'not animal or rational' produces a relation of implication, but not a conclusion of the syllogism itself.
The change illustrates that any apparent result depends on content, not syllogistic form.
4
Refute the counterclaim
The author rejects the claim that a partial affirmative conjunctive follows from the negations of the two extremes. He argues that even if the third figure produced something, it would not be the result of this particular syllogism.
This reinforces that no valid conclusion arises from two mana'at al-jam' premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كانت المنفصلتان مانعتي الجمع، لا تنتج متصلة و لا منفصلة. إذ يصدق قولنا: دائماً إما أن يكون الشيء إنساناً أو لا حيواناً. و دائماً إما أن يكون الشيء لا حيواناً أو فرساً و الحق العناد. و إذا بدلت الكبرى بقولنا: دائماً إما أن يكون الشيء لا حيواناً أو ناطقاً. كان الحق اللزوم. و قيل: تلزم متصلة موجبة جزئية من نقيضي الطرفين من الثالث والأوسط عين الأوسط. و ذلك باطل، لأنه على تقدير إنتاج الثالث لا يكون هذا نتيجة هذا القياس.
And if the two disjunctives are mana'at al-jam', they produce neither a conjunctive nor a disjunctive. For our statement is true: 'Always either the thing is a human or not an animal.' And 'Always either the thing is not an animal or a horse.' And the truth is opposition. And if we change the major to: 'Always either the thing is not an animal or an utterance.' Then the truth is implication. And it was said: 'A partial affirmative conjunctive follows from the negations of the two extremes from the third and the middle, i.e., the middle.' But that is false, because on the assumption of the production of the third, this is not a result of this syllogism.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage explains that when both premises are disjunctives (منفصلتان) that are mutually exclusive (مانعتي الجمع), they produce no valid conclusion, neither a conjunctive (متصلة) nor a disjunctive (منفصلة). The author provides an example: 'Always either X is a human or not an animal' and 'Always either X is not an animal or a horse.' The truth relation is opposition (عناد). However, if we modify the major premise to 'Always either X is not an animal or rational (ناطق)', then the relation becomes implication (لزوم). This shows that the result depends on the content. The author then refutes a claim that a partial affirmative conjunctive follows from the negations of the two extremes (i.e., from the third and the middle, being the middle itself). He states this is false because even if the third figure produced something, it would not be the result of this particular syllogism.

Key Terms

مانعتي الجمع
متصلة
منفصلة

Checkpoint

A) A conjunctive conditional.
B) A disjunctive conditional.
C) Both a conjunctive and a disjunctive.
D) None.

Key Takeaway
Two mutually exclusive disjunctive premises cannot produce a valid conclusion in any form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a pair of mana'at al-jam' disjunctives on an Islamic topic (e.g., 'Either this action is wajib or haram' and 'Either this action is haram or mubah') and explain why no conclusion follows.
?
Identify a valid disjunctive syllogism from a kalam text (e.g., from al-Sanusiyya) and compare it with the invalid case of two mana'at al-jam' premises.

Why Two Mana'at al-Khuluw Disjunctives Yield No Conclusion
Author states that two mana'at al-khuluw disjunctives produce no conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain why two mana'at al-khuluw (exhaustive but not exclusive) disjunctives do not produce a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
Two mana'at al-khuluw disjunctives force no relation between their extremes.

In disjunctive syllogisms, the type of disjunction matters. When both disjunctives are mana'at al-khuluw (the disjuncts cannot both be false but may both be true), no conclusion follows — neither implication nor opposition. The celebrated logician illustrates this with a simple example using numbers, and also refutes a faulty claim that a partial affirmative conjunctive might follow. This lesson will guide you through the reasoning so you can recognize this non-inference pattern and avoid fallacious conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two disjunctives
Locate the two disjunctive statements in the passage. They are: 'Always either four exists or ten does not exist' and 'Always either ten does not exist or five exists.' Notice that each is a mana'at al-khuluw: the disjuncts cannot both be false (exhaustive), but they might both be true.
In mana'at al-khuluw, at least one disjunct must be true; both can be true.
2
Check the middle term
The common term between the two disjunctives is 'ten does not exist' (i.e., the non-existence of ten). This serves as the middle term. In a valid disjunctive syllogism, the middle term should force a relation between the other two terms. Here, the first premise links 'four exists' and 'ten does not exist'; the second links 'ten does not exist' and 'five exists'.
3
Test for implication (luzūm)
Does the existence of four imply the existence of five? From the premises, if four exists, ten does not exist (by first premise, since if four exists, the disjunct 'ten does not exist' must be true because 'four exists' is true and they are exhaustive, but careful: in mana'at al-khuluw, if one disjunct is true, the other could be true or false. Actually, from 'four exists', we only know that 'ten does not exist' is false if four exists? Wait: The first premise 'Always either four exists or ten does not exist' means: at all times, either four exists is true OR ten does not exist is true (or both). So if four exists, then ten does not exist may be false (since both could be true? No, if four exists, then the disjunction is satisfied regardless of ten. Actually, the disjunction is true if at least one disjunct is true. So if four exists, the disjunction is true; ten does not exist could be false. So we cannot conclude that ten does not exist. Thus from four exists, we cannot infer anything about ten. Similarly, from 'ten does not exist' we cannot infer about five because of the second premise. So no implication holds.
The author explicitly says 'وجود الأربعة لا تستلزم وجود الخمسة' (the existence of four does not necessitate the existence of five).
4
Test for opposition ('inād)
Does the existence of four contradict the existence of five? The premises do not force any conflict. Both could be true simultaneously. The author says 'ولا تنافيه' (nor contradict it). Therefore, no relation of opposition follows either.
Opposition ('inād) means they cannot both be true; here they can.
5
Refute the alleged third-figure inference
The passage mentions a claim that a partial affirmative conjunctive follows from the third figure (i.e., using the opposite of the middle as a premise). The author dismisses this as already shown to be corrupt. This step is a reminder that even creative attempts to extract a conclusion fail.
This part underscores that no valid inference exists, regardless of figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و أما إذا كانتا مانعتي الخلو، فلا تلزم النتيجة أيضاً لا لزوماً ولا عناداً لصدق قولنا دائماً إما أن تكون الأربعة موجودة أو لم تكن العشرة موجودة. و دائماً إما أن لم تكن العشرة موجودة أو الخمسة موجودة. مع أن وجود الأربعة لا تستلزم وجود الخمسة و لا تنافيه. و قيل إنه تلزم متصلة موجبة جزئية، من الثالث والأوسط نقيض الأوسط. وقد بينا فساد إنتاج الثالث.
And as for if they are mana'at al-khuluw, then the result does not follow either, neither as implication nor as opposition, because our statement is true: 'Always either four exists or ten does not exist.' And 'Always either ten does not exist or five exists.' While the existence of four does not necessitate the existence of five nor contradict it. And it was said that a partial affirmative conjunctive follows, from the third and the middle being the opposite of the middle. And we have shown the corruption of the production of the third.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where both disjunctives are mana'at al-khuluw (i.e., they exclude the void, meaning the two disjuncts cannot both be false, but they may both be true). Here, also no conclusion follows, neither implication (لزوم) nor opposition (عناد). The author gives an example: 'Always either four exists or ten does not exist' and 'Always either ten does not exist or five exists.' The existence of four does not imply the existence of five, nor does it contradict it. Thus, no conclusion. The author then mentions a claim that a partial affirmative conjunctive follows from the third and the middle being the opposite of the middle, but he refutes it by stating they have already shown the corruption of the production of the third figure in this context.

Key Terms
مانعتي الخلو
لزوم
عناد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example with numbers, why does no conclusion follow?
A) Because the premises are false.
B) Because the existence of four neither implies nor contradicts the existence of five.
C) Because the premises are both particular.
D) Because the middle term is not distributed.

Key Takeaway
Two mana'at al-khuluw disjunctives, being exhaustive but not exclusive, do not force any logical relation between their extreme terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar example from fiqh: Use premises like 'Either this animal is halal or it is not slaughtered properly' and 'Either it is not slaughtered properly or it is carrion.' Explain why no conclusion about its halal status follows.
?
Given the two disjunctives: 'Either the prayer is valid or the purification is incomplete' and 'Either the purification is incomplete or the prayer is invalid,' determine whether the conclusion 'The prayer is invalid' follows. Justify your answer using the logic of mana'at al-khuluw.

Partial Negative from Mixed Disjunctive Premises
Rule for partial negative conclusion from mixed disjunctives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct partial negative conclusion when combining a negative and positive disjunctive premise, and identify which term serves as the premise based on the type of disjunction.

Core Idea
Mixed disjunctives yield a partial negative; which side becomes the premise differs by disjunction type.

We have already seen how to produce a conclusion from two positive disjunctive premises. Now the author addresses the case where one premise is negative. The conclusion is always a partial negative, but the source of that conclusion depends on whether the disjunction is mutually exclusive or exhaustive. This passage also warns of a subtle nuance regarding the scope of the terms that can affect the inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the polarity of each premise
Determine which premise is negative and which is positive. The negative premise is the one that denies a disjunct.
2
Determine the type of disjunction
Is it mana'at al-jam' (mutually exclusive – only one can be true) or mana'at al-khuluw (exhaustive – at least one is true, possibly both)?
The author uses the phrases مانعني الجمع and مانعتي الخلق (likely الخلو).
3
Apply the rule for the premise of the conclusion
If the disjunction is mana'at al-jam', the premise of the conclusion is the side (term) of the negative premise. If it is mana'at al-khuluw, the premise is the side of the positive premise.
The 'side' means the subject or predicate that appears in the conclusion, similar to the middle term in categorical syllogisms.
4
Construct the partial negative conclusion
The conclusion is a partial negative: 'Some A is not B' where A and B are the terms identified from the premise side. For example, if the negative premise says 'X is not Y', the conclusion might be 'Some X is not B' (depending on the other premise).
The exact propositional form depends on the terms.
5
Consider the caveat about generality/specificity
The author warns that the rule does not always hold exactly because the side of the negative may be more general (in mana'at al-jam') or more specific (in mana'at al-khuluw) than the side of the positive. This means the inference may not be valid if the terms are not aligned.
This nuance prevents automatic application without checking term relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و إن كانت إحداهما سالبة، تلزم سالبة جزئية، مقدمها: طرف السالبة في مانعني الجمع و طرف الموجبة في مانعتي الخلق و إلا لكذبت السالبة. و لا تلزم مقدمها بخلاف ما ذكرنا الجواز كون طرف السالبة: أتم من طرف الموجبة في مائعتي الجمع و أخص في مانعتي الخلق.
And if one of them is negative, a partial negative follows, whose premise is: the side of the negative in the case of mana'at al-jam' and the side of the positive in the case of mana'at al-khuluw, and otherwise the negative would be false. And its premise does not follow contrary to what we mentioned, because it is possible that the side of the negative is more general than the side of the positive in mana'at al-jam' and more specific in mana'at al-khuluw.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage provides a rule for when one disjunctive premise is negative and the other is positive. In that case, a partial negative (سالبة جزئية) conclusion follows. The premise (the side that appears as the premise of the conclusion) depends on the type of disjunction: If the disjunctives are mana'at al-jam' (mutually exclusive), then the premise is the side of the negative premise. If the disjunctives are mana'at al-khuluw (exhaustive), then the premise is the side of the positive premise. The author warns that the premise does not follow exactly as stated because the side of the negative may be more general than the side of the positive in mana'at al-jam', and more specific in mana'at al-khuluw. This means that the conclusion's subject and predicate might differ in scope, affecting the validity of the inference.

Key Terms
سالبة جزئية
طرف السالبة
طرف الموجبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when the disjunction is mana'at al-jam' and one premise is negative and the other positive, which side becomes the premise of the partial negative conclusion?
A) The side of the positive premise.
B) The side of the negative premise.
C) Either side depending on generality.
D) Neither side.

Key Takeaway
When one disjunctive premise is negative and the other positive, the conclusion is always a partial negative, but which term serves as its subject/predicate depends on the disjunction type, with a caveat about term generality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the disjunctive premises: 'Either the water is pure (tahir) or it is impure (najis)' (mana'at al-jam') and 'The water is not pure' (negative). Determine the conclusion and identify which term becomes the premise of the conclusion. Explain using the passage rule.
?
Find a real example from fiqh where two mutually exclusive rulings are combined, one negative and one positive. State the partial negative conclusion that would follow according to the author's rule.

Production Rules for Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author enumerates production rules for disjunctive syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given disjunctive syllogism with exclusive or exhaustive disjunctions is valid and identify the correct conclusion.

Core Idea
In disjunctive syllogisms, the type of disjunction determines the conclusion, and one premise must be affirmative.

This passage from the logical text enumerates the production rules for disjunctive syllogisms when the premises are exclusive (mani'at al-jam') or exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw). The author provides conditions for validity and shows common errors. You will learn to distinguish these types and apply the rules to Islamic law examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify disjunction types
Determine whether each premise is exclusive (both cannot be true together) or exhaustive (at least one must be true). Use the terms mani'at al-jam' and mani'at al-khuluw.
Exclusive: لا يجتمعان; Exhaustive: لا يخلوان
2
Check premise polarity
At least one premise must be affirmative. If both are negative, no conclusion follows. This is the key condition stated at the end of the passage.
3
Apply production rules for same-type disjunctions
For two exclusive disjunctions, a particular affirmative conditional follows from the negations of the two extremes. For two exhaustive disjunctions, no conclusion follows unless one is negative.
The author demonstrates with examples.
4
Apply production rules for mixed-type disjunctions
When one is exclusive and the other exhaustive, a universal conditional follows with the antecedent from the exclusive premise. If one is particular, the rule depends on which type is particular.
See the passage for details.
5
Test with an Islamic example
Construct a valid disjunctive syllogism: 'Either this act is wajib or haram' (exclusive) and 'Either it is wajib or mubah' (exhaustive). Determine the conclusion if possible.
Both premises are affirmative, so a conditional can be derived.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و إذا بتلت الكبرى بقولنا: دائماً إما أن يكون الشيء لا حيواناً أو ناطقاً. كان الحق اللزوم. و قيل: تلزم متصلة موجبة جزئية من نقيضي الطرفين من الثالث والأوسط عين الأوسط. و ذلك باطل، لأنه على تقدير إنتاج الثالث لا يكون هذا نتيجة هذا القياس. و أما إذا كانتا مانعتي الخلو، فلا تلزم النتيجة أيضاً لا لزوماً ولا عناداً لصدق قولنا دائماً إما أن تكون الأربعة موجودة أو لم تكن العشرة موجودة. و دائماً إما أن لم تكن العشرة موجودة أو الخمسة موجودة. مع أن وجود الأربعة لا تستلزم وجود الخمسة و لا تنافيه. و قيل إنه تلزم متصلة موجبة جزئية، من الثالث والأوسط نقيض الأوسط. وقد بينا فساد إنتاج الثالث. و إن كانت إحداهما سالبة، تلزم سالبة جزئية، مقدمها: طرف السالبة في مانعتي الجمع و طرف الموجبة في مانعتي الخلق و إلا لكذبت السالبة. و لا تلزم مقدمها بخلاف ما ذكرنا الجواز كون طرف السالبة أتم من طرف الموجبة في مانعتي الجمع و أخص في مانعتي الخلق. و إن كانت المنفصلتان إحداهما مانعة الجمع و الأخرى مانعة الخلق، لزمت متصلة كلية مقدمها من مانعة الجمع؛ من غير عكس و إلا لانقلبتا حقيقيتين لتساوي الأصغر و الأكبر حينئذ. و إن كانت إحداهما جزئية فإن كانت مانعة الجمع، تلزم موجبة جزئية. و إن كانت مانعة الخلق، فلا تنتج شيئاً لصدق قولنا: دائماً إما أن يكون الشيء فلكاً أو ثقيلاً. و قد يكون إما أن يكون الشيء ثقيلاً أو خفيفاً مانعة الخلق كما في العناصر. و الحق هو العناد. و إذا بدلت الصغرى بقولنا: دائماً إما أن يكون الشيء ناراً أو ثقيلاً. و الحق اللزوم. و قيل تنتج متصلة جزئية من نقيضي الأصغر و الأكبر. و قد عرفت فيما مر فساد ذلك. و إن كانت إحداهما سالبة، لم تنتج شيئاً. لأن الأخضر من نقيض الشيء قد يكذب مع نقيضه و مع لازمه المساوي. فتصدق مانعة الجمع موجبة مع مانعة الخلو سالبة مع منافاة الأصغر للأكبر تارة و مع مساواته إياه أخرى. فيلزم الاختلاف. و كذا الأتم من نقيض الشيء قد يصدق مع نقيض الشيء و لازمه المساوي. فتصدق مانعة الخلق موجبة و مانعة الجمع سالبة مع المنافاة تارة و المساواة أخرى. فقد ظهر مما ذكرنا أنه يشترط في إنتاج هذه الأقسام: إيجاب إحدى المقدمتين.
If I make the major premise by saying: 'Always, either the thing is not an animal or it is rational.' The truth is the implication. And it was said: A particular affirmative conditional follows from the two negations of the two extremes of the third and the middle, the same as the middle. That is false, because on the assumption of the production of the third, this is not a result of this syllogism. And as for if both are exhaustive disjunctions, then the result also does not follow, neither as implication nor as opposition, due to the truth of our statement: 'Always, either four exists or ten does not exist.' And 'Always, either ten does not exist or five exists.' While the existence of four does not necessitate the existence of five nor does it contradict it. And it was said that a particular affirmative conditional follows, from the third and the middle, the negation of the middle. And we have already shown the corruption of the production of the third. If one of them is negative, a particular negative follows, its antecedent: the side of the negative in exclusive disjunctions and the side of the positive in exhaustive disjunctions, otherwise the negative would be false. And its antecedent does not follow contrary to what we mentioned: the possibility that the side of the negative is more general than the side of the positive in exclusive disjunctions and more specific in exhaustive disjunctions. And if the two disjunctives are one exclusive and the other exhaustive, a universal conditional follows, its antecedent from the exclusive; without conversion, otherwise they would become real because of the equality of the minor and major then. And if one of them is particular, if it is exclusive, a particular affirmative follows. And if it is exhaustive, it produces nothing, due to the truth of our statement: 'Always, either the thing is a sphere or heavy.' And it may be that 'Always, either the thing is heavy or light' is an exhaustive disjunction as in the elements. And the truth is opposition. And if the minor is changed to our statement: 'Always, either the thing is fire or heavy.' And the truth is implication. And it was said that a particular conditional results from the negations of the minor and major. And you have already known the corruption of that in what preceded. And if one of them is negative, it produces nothing. Because the more specific of the negation of a thing may be false with its negation and with its necessary equal. So an exclusive disjunction affirmative with an exhaustive disjunction negative is true with opposition of the minor to the major sometimes and with equality of it to it other times. So difference follows. And similarly, the more general of the negation of a thing may be true with the negation of the thing and its necessary equal. So an exhaustive disjunction affirmative and an exclusive disjunction negative are true with opposition sometimes and equality other times. It has become clear from what we mentioned that it is a condition in the production of these sections: the affirmation of one of the two premises.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the production rules for disjunctive syllogisms, specifically when the disjunctions are either exclusive (mani'at al-jam') or exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw). The author first considers the case where both premises are disjunctive and gives examples to show that a certain claimed result is false. He then discusses when one premise is negative, and when the two premises are of different types. Finally, he states a general condition: one of the premises must be affirmative. Throughout, the author uses logical examples to illustrate the rules. In Islamic logic, these rules are applied in arguments such as: 'Either this act is wajib or it is haram. It is not haram. Therefore it is wajib.' (exclusive) or 'Either the world is created or eternal. It is not eternal. Therefore it is created.' (exhaustive). The passage emphasizes the importance of distinguishing between exclusive and exhaustive disjunctions, as they yield different conclusions.

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
متصلة
جزئية
كلية
سالبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In disjunctive syllogisms, when one premise is exclusive (mani'at al-jam') and the other is exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw), what type of conclusion follows?
A) No conclusion at all
B) A particular affirmative conditional
C) A universal conditional with antecedent from the exclusive premise
D) A particular negative conditional

Key Takeaway
A disjunctive syllogism requires at least one affirmative premise, and the type of disjunction determines the form of the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid disjunctive syllogism using an exclusive premise: 'Either this beverage is khamr or it is halal. It is not khamr. Therefore it is halal.' Identify the type and conclusion.

Mixed disjunctive premises: production rules
Rules for mixed disjunctive syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a valid conclusion follows from two disjunctive premises of different types (exclusive and inclusive) and identify the conclusion form when it does.

Core Idea
Not all pairs of disjunctive premises yield a conclusion; the types, quantities, and qualities must align.

Combining an exclusive disjunction (man'at al-jam') with an inclusive disjunction (man'at al-khuluw) is more delicate than using two of the same kind. This passage sets out the exact conditions under which a universal or particular conjunctive conditional (muttasila) is produced, and explains when no conclusion follows. Understanding these rules is essential for evaluating compound syllogisms in Islamic theological and legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of each disjunctive premise
Determine whether each premise is man'at al-jam' (exclusive: cannot be both true) or man'at al-khuluw (inclusive: cannot be both false). In the text, both types appear together.
Exclusive disjunction is like 'either day or night'; inclusive is like 'either water or air'.
2
Check the quantity (universal or particular) and quality (affirmative or negative)
The rules differ depending on whether the premises are universal (kulliyya) or particular (juz'iyya), and affirmative (mujiba) or negative (saliba). Note these attributes.
The text examines cases where both are universal, one is particular, and where one is negative.
3
Apply the appropriate rule from the passage
If both are universal, a universal conjunctive conditional follows (antecedent from the exclusive). If one is particular and that particular is exclusive, a particular affirmative conditional follows. If the particular is inclusive, nothing follows. If one premise is negative, nothing follows unless the negative is opposed to the affirmative (no equality).
Memorise the specific cases; the text provides examples and counterexamples.
4
Verify the general control condition
The passage ends with a summary condition: one premise must be affirmative, one universal, and if there is a negative premise, its terms must be opposed to those of the affirmative (i.e., not equal). Confirming this condition ensures validity.
The control (ضابط) is the key takeaway for quick assessment in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت المنفصلتان إحداهما مانعة الجمع والأخرى مانعة الخلو، لزمت متصلة كلية مقدمها من مانعة الجمع من غير عكس وإلا لانقلبتا حقيقيتين لتساوي الأصغر والأكبر حينئذ. وإن كانت إحداهما جزئية فإن كانت مانعة الجمع تلزم موجبة جزئية. وإن كانت مانعة الخلو فلا تنتج شيئاً لصدق قولنا: دائماً إما أن يكون الشيء فلكاً أو ثقيلاً، وقد يكون إما أن يكون الشيء ثقيلاً أو خفيفاً مانعة الخلو كما في العناصر. والحق هو العناد. وإذا بدلت الصغرى بقولنا: دائماً إما أن يكون الشيء ناراً أو ثقيلاً، والحق اللزوم. وقيل تنتج متصلة جزئية من نقيضي الأصغر والأكبر، وقد عرفت فيما مر فساد ذلك. وإن كانت إحداهما سالبة لم تنتج شيئاً ... لأن الأخضر من نقيض الشيء قد يكذب مع نقيضه ومع لازمه المساوي، فتصدق مانعة الجمع موجبة مع مانعة الخلو سالبة مع منافاة الأصغر للأكبر تارة ومع مساواته إياه أخرى، فيلزم الاختلاف. وكذا الأتم من نقيض الشيء قد يصدق مع نقيض الشيء ولازمه المساوي، فتصدق مانعة الخلو موجبة ومانعة الجمع سالبة مع المنافاة تارة والمساواة أخرى.

[الضابط لإنتاج هذا القسم]
فقد ظهر مما ذكرنا أنه يشترط في إنتاج هذه الأقسام: إيجاب إحدى المقدمتين، وكلية إحداهما، وكون السالبة منافية للموجبة إذا فرضت تلك السالبة بين طرفي الموجبة. وعلية هذا: أن السالبة لما كانت منافية للموجبة، يمتنع أن تكون بين الأصغر والأكبر مساواة، وإلا لصدق السلب بين طرفي الموجبة، لأن المسلوب عن المساوي مسلوب، وحينئذ يلزم كذب الموجبة وكذا كذب السالبة. وإذا لم تكن بينهما مساواة فإما أن تكون بينهما منافاة أو عموم، وعلى التقديرين يصدق السلب المتصل الجزئي. والله أعلم.
And if the two disjunctives are one man'at al-jam' and the other man'at al-khuluw, then a universal conjunctive conditional is necessarily implied, its antecedent from man'at al-jam', without conversion, otherwise they would become true disjunctives due to the equality of the minor and major then. And if one of them is particular, then if it is man'at al-jam', a particular affirmative is implied. And if it is man'at al-khuluw, nothing is produced, because our statement: 'Always either the thing is a celestial body or heavy' is true, and it may be 'Either the thing is heavy or light' as a man'at al-khuluw as in the elements. And the truth is the opposition. And if the minor is substituted with our statement: 'Always either the thing is fire or heavy', and the truth is the implication. And it was said that a particular conjunctive conditional is produced from the negations of the minor and major, and you have already known the corruption of that. And if one of them is negative, nothing is produced... because the green of the negation of a thing may be false with its negation and with its equal necessary, so man'at al-jam' affirmative with man'at al-khuluw negative is true with the opposition of the minor and major sometimes and with its equality another time, so difference results. And likewise the most perfect of the negation of a thing may be true with the negation of the thing and its equal necessary, so man'at al-khuluw affirmative and man'at al-jam' negative are true with opposition sometimes and equality another time.

[The control for the production of this section]
So it has become clear from what we mentioned that the production of these sections requires: affirmativeness of one of the two premises, and universality of one of them, and that the negative is opposed to the affirmative if that negative is assumed between the two terms of the affirmative. And the reason for this is: since the negative is opposed to the affirmative, it is impossible for there to be equality between the minor and major, otherwise the negation between the two terms of the affirmative would be true, because what is negated from the equal is negated, and then the falsity of the affirmative and likewise the falsity of the negative would follow. And if there is no equality between them, then either there is opposition or generality between them, and in both cases the particular connected negative is true. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of syllogistic production when two disjunctive premises are of different types: one is man' al-jam' (prevention of coexistence, i.e., exclusive disjunction) and the other is man' al-khuluw (prevention of emptiness, i.e., inclusive disjunction). The author examines various cases based on whether the premises are universal or particular, affirmative or negative. He states that if both disjunctives are universal, a universal conjunctive conditional is produced with its antecedent from the exclusive disjunction, without conversion; otherwise, if conversion were applied, the disjunctives would become true (i.e., lose their exclusive/inclusive character) due to the equality of the minor and major terms. If one premise is particular, then if the particular is the exclusive disjunction, a particular affirmative conjunctive conditional results; but if the particular is the inclusive disjunction, nothing is produced, as shown by the example: 'Always either the thing is a celestial body or heavy' (a true universal exclusive) and 'It may be either heavy or light' (a particular inclusive) – no conclusion follows. The truth in such case is 'opposition' (i.e., the conclusion is contradictory). If the minor is changed to 'Always either the thing is fire or heavy', then the truth is the implication (i.e., the conjunctive conditional follows). He rejects the claim that a particular conjunctive conditional could be produced from the negations of the minor and major terms. If one premise is negative, nothing is produced; he gives an argument using the example of 'green' (something that is neither the original thing nor its negation) and 'most perfect' (something that includes both the original and its negation) to show that the truth-status of the premises can vary depending on whether the terms are opposed or equal. Finally, he provides a general control (ضابط) for the production of such mixed disjunctive syllogisms: one premise must be affirmative, one must be universal, and if a negative premise is present, its terms must be opposed to those of the affirmative premise (i.e., no equality). He proves this condition by showing that if equality were possible, both premises would be false. If no equality, then either opposition or generality holds, and a particular negative conjunctive conditional is true throughout. The passage is central to understanding the rules of compound disjunctive syllogisms in classical Islamic logic.

B) A particular conjunctive conditional.

When combining exclusive and inclusive disjunctive premises, the only guaranteed product is a universal conjunctive conditional from two universal premises; other combinations require careful checking of quantity, quality, and opposition.

The Syllogism

Disjunctive Syllogism with Partial Middle Term
Author lists conditions for a productive disjunctive syllogism with a partial middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and verify the four conditions for a productive disjunctive syllogism when the middle term is an incomplete part of each premise.

Core Idea
Four conditions (affirmative, inclusive, universal, productive composition) guarantee a valid disjunctive syllogism with a partial middle term.

Disjunctive syllogisms often involve a middle term that is not the whole subject or predicate but only a part. The author specifies conditions for such a syllogism to yield a necessary conclusion. These conditions ensure that the premises are not empty, that one is universal, and that the partial terms combine into a valid logical figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the partial middle term
In the disjunctive premises, the middle term (al-awsat) is incomplete (juz' ghayr tamm), meaning it does not form the entire subject or predicate but only a component. Locate the shared term or part that appears in both premises.
For example, if premises are 'Every A is either B or C' and 'Every A is either D or E', the middle term is 'A' which is partial because it is only the subject, not the entire predicate.
2
Check affirmativeness of premises
Both premises must be affirmative (إيجاب المقدمتين). Negative disjunctives (e.g., 'No A is B or C') are not allowed. Verify that each premise asserts a positive relation.
This condition excludes exclusive disjunctions that include negation.
3
Check prevention of emptiness
Each premise must have man' al-khuluw – the disjunction is inclusive, meaning at least one of the two parts is true; both cannot be false. Ensure that the disjunction covers all possibilities without leaving a gap.
An exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam') would not satisfy this condition.
4
Check universality of one premise
At least one of the two premises must be universal (كلية إحداهما). This means it applies to every individual of the subject class. If both are particular, the argument fails.
Universality ensures a strong connection between the middle term and the conclusion.
5
Check productive composition of the two shares
The two shares (المشاركان) – the partial middle terms from each premise – must combine to form a productive composition (تأليف منتج). This means the combination yields a valid syllogistic figure (e.g., first figure) that necessarily leads to a conclusion.
If the partial terms do not align in a valid deductive pattern, no conclusion follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني [من منفصلتين]
أن يكون الأوسط جزءاً غير تام من كل واحدة منها
[شرائط إنتاج هذا القسم]
و شرط إنتاجه:
إيجاب المقدمتين.
و منع الخلو فيهما.
وكلية إحداهما.
و اشتمال المنشاركين على تأليف منتج.
The second section [of two disjunctives]: that the middle term is an incomplete part of each one of them. [Conditions for the production of this section]: And the condition for its production: affirmativeness of the two premises. And prevention of emptiness in them. And universality of one of them. And the inclusion of the two shares in a productive composition.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This brief passage introduces a second type of disjunctive syllogism where the middle term (the common element) is not a complete part but only a partial component (جزء غير تام) of each disjunctive premise. For such a syllogism to be productive, the author gives four conditions: (1) both premises must be affirmative (إيجاب المقدمتين), meaning no negative disjunctives; (2) there must be prevention of emptiness (منع الخلو) in both premises – i.e., each premise must be an inclusive disjunction that does not allow both parts to be false; (3) one of the premises must be universal (كلية إحداهما); and (4) the two shares (المشاركين) must be combined in a productive composition (تأليف منتج), meaning that the partial middle terms in both premises must together form a valid syllogistic figure. These conditions ensure that a valid conclusion follows. The passage is typically explained in the context of complex disjunctive reasoning in Islamic logic, often illustrated with examples from natural philosophy where categories like elements have partial natures.

Conditions and Five Methods of the Second Division of Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author states conditions for production and enumerates five methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four conditions for a productive disjunctive syllogism of the second division and enumerate the five methods (masalik) of participation between the parts of the two disjunctive premises.

Core Idea
Five methods of part-sharing between two disjunctives yield valid compound syllogisms under four conditions.

This passage introduces the second division of disjunctive syllogisms, where both premises are disjunctive and the middle term is an incomplete part of each. The author lays out the necessary conditions for such a syllogism to produce a conclusion and then categorizes five distinct ways the parts of the two premises can relate. Understanding these methods is crucial for constructing correct compound arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division
Note that this is the second division of disjunctive syllogisms, where the middle term is an incomplete part of each disjunctive premise. The two premises are both disjunctive (munfasila).
2
List the four conditions
Find and memorize the four conditions for production: both premises must be affirmative (ijab), there must be prevention of vacuity (man' al-khuluw) meaning the disjuncts are exhaustive, one premise must be universal (kulliyya), and the two premises together must contain a productive composition (ta'lif muntij).
The condition 'prevention of vacuity' ensures no third option is left out.
3
Understand the conclusion structure
The conclusion is a disjunction that prevents vacuity (mani'at al-khulwa). It can be derived from the essence of a part that does not participate in the sharing, or from the result of the composition (or from multiple compositions if more than one).
4
Enumerate the five methods
Learn the five methods (masalik) based on how many parts of one premise share with parts of the other: (1) one part with one part, (2) one part with all parts, (3) each part with one part, (4) each part with each part, (5) one part with all parts and the remaining each with one part.
The four figures of categorical syllogism can appear in each method.
5
Recognize the minor vs major distinction
The minor premise is distinguished from the major by considering which two parts are the ones that participate (the participating parts). This determines the subject and predicate roles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني [من منفصلتين]
أن يكون الأوسط جزءاً غير تام من كل واحدة منها
[شرائط إنتاج هذا القسم]
و شرط إنتاجه:
إيجاب المقدمتين.
و منع الخلو فيهما.
وكلية إحداهما
و اشتمال المشاركين على تأليف منتج.
النتيجة مانعة الخلوة
و من عين ما لا يشارك - إن كان - و من نتيجة التأليف ، واحدة كانت أو أكثر،
و إلا فمن نتائج التأليفات.
و هذا القسم خمسة مسالك: لأنه أما أن يشارك:
١ جزء من إحدى المقدمتين جزءاً واحداً من الأخرى،
٢. أو جميع أجزاء الأخرى،
.. أوكل واحدٍ واحداً من الأخرى،
.. أو كلُّ واحدٍ كل واحدٍ من الأخرى،
.. أو واحدٌ جميع أجزاء الأخرى و الباقي كلُّ واحدٍ منه واحداً من الأخرى.
و تنعقد الأشكال الأربعة في كل مسلك منها.
و تتميز الصغرى عن الكبرى باعتبار الجزئين المتشاركين.
مثال [المسلك] الأول قولنا:
١ كل القاب و إنتاج. (هذه القضية ليست بمنفصلة بل حملية مرددة المحمول. فهذا المثال ليس من القس المؤلّف من منفصلتين و الأنسب عنا المقام تبديل الصغرى و النتيجة بمنفصلتين: إنما كل آب و إما كل ج ... و إما كل ده و إنما كل وز. إنما كل آب و إنما كل ج - و إما كل و ز.)
The second division [of two disjunctives]: that the middle term is an incomplete part of each one of them. [Conditions for production of this division:] Its condition for production: affirmation of the two premises, prevention of vacuity in them, and universality of one of them, and the two participants contain a productive composition. The result: preventing vacuity, and from the essence of what does not participate – if there is – and from the result of the composition, whether one or more; otherwise from the results of compositions. This division has five methods: because it either participates: (1) a part of one premise with one part of the other; (2) or all parts of the other; (3) or each one with one of the other; (4) or each one with each one of the other; (5) or one with all parts of the other and the remaining each one with one of the other. The four figures are formed in each method. The minor is distinguished from the major by consideration of the two participating parts. Example of the first method: our saying: 'Every A is B and productive.' (This proposition is not disjunctive but categorical with a disjunctive predicate. So this example is not from the composition of two disjunctives; the appropriate here is to replace the minor and the result with two disjunctives: 'Every A is either B or every J... and every D is either H or every W Z. Every A is either B and every J - and either every W Z.')
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage discusses the second division of disjunctive syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i munfasil). In this division, the middle term is an incomplete part of each of the two disjunctive premises. The author first lists four conditions for the syllogism to be productive: (1) both premises must be affirmative, (2) there must be 'prevention of vacuity' (man' al-khuluw) between them, meaning the disjuncts in the two premises must be mutually exclusive and exhaustive?; (3) one premise must be universal, and (4) the two 'participants' (the two disjunctive premises) must contain a composition that yields a conclusion. Then the conclusion is described as 'preventing vacuity' (man'at al-khulwah) – that is, the conclusion itself must be a disjunction that prevents vacuity. The conclusion can be derived either from the essence of what does not participate (if there is such a part) or from the result of the composition. The author then enumerates five possible modes (masalik) of participation between the parts of the two disjunctive premises: (1) one part of the minor premise shares with one part of the major; (2) one part shares with all parts of the other; (3) each part of one shares with one part of the other; (4) each part of one shares with each part of the other; (5) one part shares with all parts of the other, and the remaining parts each with one part of the other. In each of these five methods, the four figures of the categorical syllogism can be formed. The distinction between minor and major premise is based on the two participating parts. Finally, the author gives an example for the first method: 'Every A is B and productive' – but then notes that this example is incorrect because the premise is not truly disjunctive but a categorical with a disjunctive predicate (hamliyya muraddat al-mahmul). The correct form should use two disjunctive premises. The example is incomplete due to OCR issues.

Key Takeaway

The First Path in Composition of Disjunctive Syllogisms: Distinguishing True Disjunctives from Categoricals with Disjunctive Predicates
Author provides an example of the first path where one part of a premise shares with one part of the other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a proposition is a genuine disjunctive or a categorical with disjunctive predicate, and apply the first path of composition to construct a valid syllogism.

Core Idea
The first path of composition combines one shared part; not every 'either-or' proposition is a true disjunctive.

This lesson examines a classical example meant to illustrate the first path of composition in disjunctive syllogisms. The commentator warns that the initial example is not a true disjunctive but a categorical with disjunctive predicate. We will learn to distinguish these and correctly apply the composition rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine if a proposition is a genuine disjunctive (منفصلة) or a categorical with disjunctive predicate (حملية مرددة المحمول). A genuine disjunctive has disjuncts that are complete propositions, while a categorical with disjunctive predicate has a predicate that is a disjunction of terms. For example, 'Every A is either B or C' is categorical if B and C are terms, not propositions.
2
Recognize the first path condition
The first path requires that one part of one premise shares with one part of the other premise. If you have 'Every A is either B or C' and 'Every D is either B or E', then B is shared. The conclusion will combine the unshared parts.
3
Correct the example
The given example 'Every A is either B or productive' is not disjunctive but categorical. To make it a genuine disjunctive syllogism, replace the minor and conclusion with true disjunctives: e.g., 'Every A is either B or every J' and 'Every D is either H or every W Z'. This yields a conclusion with three parts.
The corrected form uses complete propositions as disjuncts.
4
Verify the composition rule
The proof states that the actual state cannot be free from the syllogism producing the result of composition and one of the other two. This means that in the first path, the shared part yields a composite conclusion that includes the participating parts and adds the non-participating parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال [المسلك] الأول قولنا: ١ كل القاب و إنتاج. ١. قمر كشف الأسرار ص ۳۶۸ س ۱۰۰۷ و مطالع الأنوار الواقع الأسرار في شرح مطالع الأنوار ص ۶۵۰) فإن بين البيانات الثلاثة فروقاً. ٢ أو واحد واحد أو ص. كشف الأسرار ص ۳۶۳ - ۱۵ إلى ص ۳۶۴ س ۲. . كشف الأسرار ص ۳۶۴ س ۲-۴ ه. كل آ إماب وإنتاج: هذه القضية ليست بمنفصلة بل حملية مرددة المحمول. فهذا المثال ليس من القس المؤلّف من منفصلتين و الأنسب عنا المقام تبديل الصغرى و النتيجة بمنفصلتين: إنما كل آب و إما كل ج ... و إما كل ده و إنما كل وز. إنما كل آب و إنما كل ج - و إما كل و ز. ينتج: و «إما كل ج د و إما كل هز»، كل آإتاب و إماد و إما كل هـ زه. و النتيجة ثلاثة أجزاء. برهانه أن الواقع لا يخلو عن القياس المنتج لنتيجة التأليف و عن أحد الآخرين. ينتج
Example of the first path: our statement: 1. Every A is either B or productive? 1. Qamar Kashf al-Asrar p. 368 line 1007 and Matali' al-Anwar ... the differences between the three statements. 2. Or one one or ?. Kashf al-Asrar p. 363-15 to p. 364 line 2. Kashf al-Asrar p. 364 lines 2-4. H. Every A is either B and productive: This proposition is not disjunctive but categorical with disjunctive predicate. So this example is not of the type composed of two disjunctives. More appropriate for this context is to replace the minor and the conclusion with two disjunctives: Every A is either B or every J ... and Every D is either H or every W Z. Every A is either B and every J - and every W Z. It yields: and 'Every J is either D or every H Z', every A is either B and either D and either every H Z. And the conclusion has three parts. Its proof: The actual state cannot be free from the syllogism producing the result of composition and from one of the other two. It yields.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage provides an example for the first path of composition in conditional syllogisms. The author begins with an example that appears to involve disjunctive propositions but then comments that the given proposition 'كل آ إماب وإنتاج' (every A is either B or productive) is not truly a disjunctive (منفصلة) but rather a categorical proposition with a disjunctive predicate (حملية مرددة المحمول). Therefore, this example does not belong to the type composed of two disjunctives. The commentator suggests a more appropriate formulation: replace the minor premise and conclusion with genuine disjunctives, such as: 'Every A is either B or every J' and 'Every D is either H or every W Z', yielding a conclusion with three parts: 'Every A is either B and either D and either every H Z'. The proof states that the actual state of affairs cannot be free from the syllogism that produces the result of composition (i.e., the linking of the participating parts) and one of the other two non-participating parts. This illustrates how the first path operates: one part of one premise (e.g., 'A is B') shares with one part of the other premise, leading to a conclusion that combines the participating parts and includes the non-participating parts.

Key Terms
المنفصلة
الحملية المرددة المحمول

The Ambiguous Conclusion in the Second Path of Conditional Syllogisms
Commentator exemplifies and analyses the second path's ambiguous conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ambiguity in a conditional syllogism conclusion and calculate the six possible logical forms resulting from two meanings and three disjunction types.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism's conclusion can be ambiguous, yielding six possible logical forms.

In this passage, the commentator dissects an example from the second path of composition in conditional syllogisms. The premises seem straightforward, but the conclusion is deliberately ambiguous. Understanding this ambiguity is essential for correctly evaluating the validity of such syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the example carefully
Locate the given premises: 'Every A is either B and productive' and 'Every J is either D and H'. Identify the conclusion: 'Every A is either Q and D and H'. Notice the symbolic notation and variable letters.
The variables A, B, J, D, H, Q are placeholders for terms in logic.
2
Note the ambiguity
The commentator explicitly says the conclusion is ambiguous between two meanings. Look for these two meanings in the passage: one is 'Every A is either Q or D and every D...' and the other is similar but with different distribution.
The ambiguity arises from the arrangement of the disjunctive parts.
3
Enumerate the six possible conclusions
Each of the two meanings can be a real disjunction (haqiqiyya), exclusive of conjunction (mani'at al-jam'), or exclusive of emptiness (mani'at al-khulwa). Multiply: 2 meanings × 3 types = 6 results. Verify the text: 'فالحاصل ست نتائج'.
This multiplication shows the importance of specifying the disjunction type.
4
Understand the refinement
Al-Khunaji and the author favor the first meaning as exclusive of emptiness (mani'at al-khulwa), but the commentator argues both meanings should be exclusive of emptiness due to entailment. This is a critical refinement of the logical analysis.
The refinement ensures the conclusion is logically tight.

This conclusion is ambiguous between two meanings. 1. Commentary
This passage exemplifies the second path of composition in conditional syllogisms. The given premises are 'Every A is either B and productive' and 'Every J is either D and either H', and the conclusion is 'Every A is either Q and either D and either H'. The commentator immediately notes that this conclusion is ambiguous between two meanings (مرددة بين معنيين). These two meanings correspond to different types of disjunction: each can be a real disjunction (حقيقية), an exclusive disjunction of conjunction (مانعة الجمع), or an exclusive disjunction of emptiness (مانعة الخلوة). The combination of two possible meanings and three types yields six possible conclusions (فالحاصل ست نتائج). The author sides with al-Khunaji (الخونجي) that the first meaning is exclusive of emptiness (مانعاً الخلق), though the correct view is that both are exclusive of emptiness due to their mutual entailment. The passage then explains the 'syllogism producing the result of composition' as the coupling between the participating parts (e.g., A J from the minor and J D from the major) and clarifies that 'one of the other two' refers to the non-participating parts (e.g., A B from the minor and H Z from the major). Finally, it notes that this syllogism is composed of two categorical propositions with disjunctive predicates, not genuine disjunctives, and suggests a more appropriate example using disjunctives.

How the Second Method of Conditional Syllogisms Produces a Disjunctive Conclusion
Author provides an example of the second method for conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion of a conditional syllogism using the second method when given one categorical and one disjunctive premise.

Core Idea
The second method turns a categorical and a disjunctive premise into a new disjunctive conclusion via shared term composition.

Conditional syllogisms often involve disjunctive premises. The second method (al-maslak al-thānī) uses a categorical premise and a disjunctive premise where one disjunct shares a term with the categorical. By composing through that shared term, we derive a categorical conclusion from that disjunct, then attach the remaining disjunct(s) to form a full disjunctive conclusion. This method appears in logic texts to handle mixed premises efficiently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
Locate the universal affirmative categorical premise (e.g., 'Every A is B'). This is the simple, non-disjunctive part.
In the example, it is 'كل آ ب' (Every A is B).
2
Identify the disjunctive premise and its disjuncts
Find the disjunctive premise (e.g., 'Either every C is D or every H is Z'). Note the two disjuncts.
The example uses 'إما كل ج د و إما كل ه ز' (Either every C is D or every H is Z).
3
Find the shared term between the categorical premise and one disjunct
Check if the categorical premise's predicate (or subject) matches a term in one disjunct. In the example, 'B' appears in the categorical premise and also in the disjunct 'either B is C' (implied by the composition). Actually the shared term is B, connecting 'Every A is B' and the disjunct 'B is C'.
The text says the syllogism produces 'the result of composition' (natījat al-ta'līf) using the middle term.
4
Compose through the shared term to derive a categorical conclusion from that disjunct
If the categorical premise is 'Every A is B' and the disjunct is 'Every B is C', then by Barbara we get 'Every A is C'. This is the categorical part of the final conclusion.
This step assumes the disjunct is universal affirmative; other forms would follow analogous rules.
5
Attach the remaining disjunct to form the disjunctive conclusion
Take the categorical conclusion from step 4 and disjoin it with the other disjunct(s) from the original disjunctive premise. The result is a disjunction between the composed categorical and the untouched disjunct.
In the example: 'Every A is C' (from composition) and 'Every H is Z' (remaining disjunct) give 'Every A is D or every H is Z' (since C=D in the example? Actually the text has 'كل ج د' so C and D are used. So the composed is 'Every A is D' and the other is 'Every H is Z'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينتج: و «إما كل ج د و إما كل ه ز»، كل آ ب و إما د و إما كل ه ز. و النتيجة ثلاثة أجزاء. برهانه أن الواقع لا يخلو عن القياس المنتج لنتيجة التأليف و عن أحد الآخرين. مثال المسلك الثاني: كل آ ب و إما ب ج، و كل ج د و إما ه ز، ينتج: كل آ د و إما ه ز.
It produces: 'Either every C is D or every H is Z', every A B and either D and every H Z. The conclusion has three parts. Its proof is that the actual situation does not lack the syllogism that produces the result of composition and one of the other two. Example of the second path: Every A is B and either B is C, and every C is D or every H is Z, so it produces: Every A is D or every H is Z.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage defines the second method (maslak) for producing a conclusion in a conditional syllogism. The syllogism has two premises: one categorical ('Every A is B') and one disjunctive ('Either every C is D or every H is Z'). The conclusion is a disjunction ('Every A is D or every H is Z'). The proof states that the actual situation must consist of either the syllogism producing the composition (i.e., combining the common terms) or one of the other two (the non-common parts). The example clarifies how the middle term (B) links the categorical with one disjunct to generate the conclusion.

Key Terms

القياس المنتج لنتيجة التأليف
الجزآن الغير المتشاركان

Key Takeaway
The second method of conditional syllogisms produces a disjunctive conclusion by composing a categorical premise with one disjunct through a shared term and then disjoining with the remaining disjunct(s).

Example of the Third Path in Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author presents an example of the third path producing two conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the two conclusions from a pair of disjunctive premises using the third method.

Core Idea
Two disjunctive premises can yield two distinct conclusions by combining terms transitively.

In this lesson, we examine a concrete example of the third method (al-maslak al-thālith) for conditional syllogisms. The author presents two disjunctive premises: 'Either every A is B or every C is D' and 'Either every B is H or every D is Z'. From these, the syllogism produces two conclusions, each a three-part disjunction. This example demonstrates how the method works when both premises are disjunctive and how the terms are linked. Let's walk through the derivation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the two disjunctive propositions. Premise 1: Either every A is B or every C is D. Premise 2: Either every B is H or every D is Z.
2
Recognize the overlapping terms
Notice that 'B' appears in the first disjunct of both premises, and 'D' appears in the second disjunct of both premises. This allows transitive chains: A→B→H and C→D→Z.
3
Derive the first conclusion
Combine the first disjunct of premise 1 (A is B) with the first disjunct of premise 2 (B is H) and add the transitive result from the second disjuncts (C is D and D is Z gives C is Z). This yields: Either every A is B, or every B is H, or every C is Z.
4
Derive the second conclusion
Take the transitive result from the first disjuncts (A is B and B is H gives A is H) and combine it with the second disjunct of premise 1 (C is D) and the second disjunct of premise 2 (D is Z). This yields: Either every A is H, or every C is D, or every D is Z.
5
Verify the two conclusions
Check that both conclusions are distinct and follow from the premises. The first conclusion preserves the first disjunct of premise 1 and the first disjunct of premise 2, plus the derived C is Z. The second preserves the second disjuncts and the derived A is H.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال المسلك الثالث: إما كل آ ب و إما كل ج د، و إما كل ب ه و إما كل د ز، ينتج نتيجتين: إحداهما: إما كل آ ب و إما كل ب ه و إما كل ج ز. الثانية: إما كل آ ه و إما كل ج د و إما كل د ز.
Example of the third path: Either every A is B or every C is D, and either every B is H or every D is Z, it produces two conclusions: one of them: Either every A is B or every B is H or every C is Z. The second: Either every A is H or every C is D or every D is Z.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage illustrates the third method (maslak al-thālith) for conditional syllogisms. Here both premises are disjunctive: the first premise states 'Either every A is B or every C is D', and the second premise states 'Either every B is H or every D is Z'. From these, two different conclusions are derived. The first conclusion combines the first disjunct of the first premise with the first disjunct of the second premise and the second disjunct of the first premise with the second disjunct of the second premise? Actually it links A-B from first premise, B-H from second premise, and C-Z from mixing? The text shows the conclusions as three-part disjunctions. This method shows how to handle two disjunctive premises with overlapping terms.

Key Terms
النتيجة (al-natīja): The conclusion(s) produced by the syllogism; here two conclusions are possible from the same pair of premises.
القضية المنفصلة (al-qaḍiyya al-munfaṣila): A disjunctive proposition (either...or) used as a premise in this conditional syllogism. [Note: Standard usage in Mantiq.]
المسلك الثالث (al-maslak al-thālith): The third method in conditional syllogisms, which uses two disjunctive premises and produces two conclusions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
A pair of disjunctive premises can produce two distinct conclusions, each combining the terms in different chains.

Examples of the Third, Fourth, and Fifth Maslak of Compound Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author gives examples of three compound disjunctive syllogisms producing two conclusions each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the two conclusions from a compound disjunctive syllogism following the third maslak, and describe the patterns for the fourth and fifth maslaks.

Core Idea
Compound disjunctive syllogisms produce multiple conclusions by systematically combining parts of the premises.

In the previous lessons, you learned about compound syllogisms and their maslaks. Here, the author provides concrete examples of the third, fourth, and fifth patterns. Understanding these examples will allow you to generate the correct conclusions when faced with two disjunctive premises. The key is to identify which parts of the premises are combined in each conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Parse the disjuncts of the third maslak
Identify the two premises: (1) إما كل آب و إما كل جده, (2) إما كل به و إما كل دز. Each is a disjunction of two categorical propositions.
Pay attention to the Arabic letters: آب = every A is B, جده = every C is D, etc.
2
Locate the two conclusions in the text
The first conclusion is: إما كل آب و إما كل به و إما كل جز. The second is: إما كل آه و إما كل جده و إما كل دز. Notice that each conclusion has three disjuncts.
The conclusions are not simple combinations; they involve a new term (جز, آه) derived from the original premises.
3
Discern the pattern for the third maslak
The first conclusion takes the first disjunct of each premise and adds a third disjunct that combines the subject of the second disjunct of the first premise with the predicate of the second disjunct of the second premise. The second conclusion takes the second disjunct of each premise and adds a combination of the first disjunct of the first premise's subject with the second disjunct of the second premise's predicate.
This pattern is explained by the author as yielding two results.
4
Examine the fourth and fifth maslaks
The fourth maslak involves four premises and yields four-part conclusions. The fifth maslak includes a shared term in the second premise. Read the examples and identify how the conclusions are formed.
The author states that the proof for all is the same as previously given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال المسلك الثالث: إما كل آب و إما كل جده، و إما كل به و إما كل دز، ينتج نتيجتين: إحداهما: إما كل آب و إما كل به و إما كل ج ز، الثانية: إما كل آه و إما كل جد و إما كل دز.
مثال المسلك الرابع: إما كل آب و إما كل ب ج، و إما كل ج أو إما كل بد، و إما بعض ب ج و إما كل آد، و إما كل ب آ و إما بعض ج د، و النتيجة أربعة أجزاء من نتائج التأليفات.
مثال المسلك الخامس: إما كل آب و إما كل ج، و إما كل ده و إما كل داء، ينتج نتيجتين: إحداهما: إما كل آب و إما كل ج ه و إما كل ج، الثانية: إما بعض بد و إما كل ج أو إما كل ده، و النتيجة مركبة من الجزء المشارك لأحدهما و من نتيجتي التأليفين. و البرهان في الجميع ما مر.
Example of the third path: Either every A is B or every C is D, and either every B is E or every D is Z, it yields two results: one: either every A is B or every B is E or every C is Z; second: either every A is H or every C is D or every D is Z. Example of the fourth path: Either every A is B or every B is C, and either every C is D or every B is D, and either some B is C or every A is D, and either every B is A or some C is D, and the result is four parts from the results of the compositions. Example of the fifth path: Either every A is B or every C is D, and either every D is E or every D is F, it yields two results: one: either every A is B or every C is E or every C is D; second: either some B is D or every C is D or every D is E, and the result is composed of the part shared by one of them and from the two results of the two compositions. And the proof in all is as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage provides examples of the third, fourth, and fifth 'maslak' (paths) of compound syllogisms. Each example consists of two conditional disjunctive premises (using 'إما...وإما', meaning 'either...or') and produces two conclusions. The third path: the premises are (1) either every A is B or every C is D, (2) either every B is E or every D is Z. The first conclusion combines the first part of the first premise with the first part of the second, plus the second part of the first; the second conclusion combines the second part of the first premise with the second part of the second, plus the first part of the first. The fourth path involves four premises yielding a four-part conclusion. The fifth path includes a shared term in the second premise and yields a conclusion combining the shared part with the two results. The author notes that the demonstration for all these is the same as previously given.

Non-necessity of Man' al-Jam' in Compound Syllogisms
Author explains that man' al-jam' is not required in the conclusions of these five sections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a compound syllogism's conclusion does not require exclusive disjunction (man' al-jam') and identify how man' al-jam' can follow indirectly from the contradictories of the parts.

Core Idea
Compound syllogism conclusions may be broader than premises; exclusivity is not required.

In classical Islamic logic, compound syllogisms often involve disjunctive premises. A natural assumption is that the conclusion must also exhibit exclusive disjunction (man' al-jam'). This passage clarifies that such exclusivity is not obligatory because the conclusion can be more comprehensive than the premise composition. However, the author notes that if we consider the contradictories of the parts of the conclusion, man' al-jam' does arise indirectly, through the transformation of man' al-khulu. This nuance is crucial for correctly evaluating compound arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
The author states that man' al-jam' is not obligatory in the conclusions of the five types of compound syllogisms discussed. The claim is that the conclusion does not need to have mutually exclusive disjuncts.
The 'five sections' refer to the types of compound syllogisms covered in the preceding part of the text.
2
Understand the rationale
The reason given is that the conclusion may be 'more complete' (أتم) than the premise composition. That is, the conclusion can be true without being solely dependent on the specific conjunction of premises. It might hold for additional reasons, so it need not be exclusive.
This prevents a premature rejection of a valid conclusion simply because it lacks exclusivity.
3
Examine the example
The author gives: 'Always either every human is X or a brayer, and always either every brayer is laughing or every inanimate is not laughing.' Here the conclusion is true even though the disjunction is not exclusive. Work through the example to see that the conclusion holds without man' al-jam'.
The exact term 'لانا هقاً' is unclear, but the logical point remains: the disjunction is not exclusive.
4
Note the indirect exception
The author adds that if we take the contradictories of the parts of the conclusion, then man' al-jam' does follow, but only indirectly through the conversion (انقلاب) of man' al-khulu (exhaustive disjunction) into man' al-jam'. This is not intrinsic to the original conclusion.
Man' al-khulu means the disjuncts cannot both be false; its convert (contrapositive) yields exclusivity.
5
Induce a general ruling
From induction, the author observes that in some compound syllogisms, the parts of one premise share a term together, and in others, the parts of each premise share terms. This affects how the conclusion is structured.
This helps in classifying compound syllogisms for analysis.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage discusses the logical property of 'man' al-jam'' (prevention of conjunction) in the conclusions of compound syllogisms. The author asserts that it is not obligatory for the conclusion to exhibit man' al-jam'; i.e., the disjunctive parts of the conclusion do not need to be mutually exclusive. The reason is that the conclusion may be more comprehensive than the premise composition, so it can be true without the premise's specific combination. An example is given: 'Either every human is X or a brayer, and either every brayer is laughing or every inanimate is not laughing' – the conclusion holds true even if the disjunction is not exclusive. However, the author notes that from the contradictories of the parts of the conclusion, man' al-jam' does follow, but only indirectly through the conversion of 'man' al-khulu' (prevention of emptiness) into man' al-jam', not intrinsically. The passage then gives a general ruling derived from induction: sometimes the parts of one premise share a term together, sometimes the parts of each premise share.

Critique of Shaykh's Deduction from Disjunctives
Critique of a deduction attempt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's critique of the Shaykh's deduction and explain why disjunctives are considered categoricals in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Disjunctives are actually categoricals—no special deduction is needed.

The author critiques a logical move by the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) who attempted to deduce a categorical proposition from two disjunctive propositions using the second figure. The author argues this is unnecessary because disjunctives are already categorical in nature. This lesson dissects that critique and clarifies the relationship between disjunctive and categorical propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shaykh's deduction
The Shaykh claims that from two disjunctive premises in the second figure, a categorical conclusion follows. Locate the example: 'Every A is either B or C; Nothing from D is either B or C; therefore Nothing from A is D.'
2
Recognize the author's objection
The author states this is an error. Understand why: the deduction implies disjunctives are a separate category requiring special treatment.
3
Understand the author's claim
The author asserts that disjunctives are 'similar to categoricals, indeed in reality they are them.' This means disjunctives are already categorical in form—no transformation is needed.
4
Reflect on the logical insight
Consider why someone might treat disjunctives distinctly. The author's insight simplifies logic: treating disjunctives as categoricals allows direct application of categorical syllogistic rules.
This aligns with later Islamic logicians who analyze disjunctives as compound categoricals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استنتاج الشيخ حملية من منفصلتين. واستنتج الشيخ من الشكل الثاني حملية كقولنا: كل أ إما ب و إما ج، ولا شيء من د إما ب و إما ج، ينتج لا شيء من أ د. خطأ الشيخ في استنتاج الحملية من المنفصلتين. وأنت تعلم كون المنفصلتين شبيبتين بالحمليتين بل بالحقيقة هما هما.
The Shaykh's deduction of a categorical from two disjunctives. And the Shaykh deduced from the second figure a categorical, such as our saying: 'Every A is either B or C, and nothing from D is either B or C', it follows that 'nothing from A is D'. Error of the Shaykh in deducing a categorical from two disjunctives. And you know that the two disjunctives are similar to the categoricals, indeed in reality they are them.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a logical claim made by 'the Shaykh' (likely Avicenna). The author points out an error: the Shaykh attempted to deduce a categorical proposition (hamliyya) from two exclusive disjunctive propositions (munfasilatayn), specifically using the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The example given is: 'Every A is either B or C' and 'Nothing from D is either B or C' yielding 'Nothing from A is D'. The author then declares this a mistake, asserting that the two disjunctives are actually similar to categoricals – indeed, they are essentially categoricals. This means the Shaykh's deduction is redundant; disjunctives can already be treated as categoricals without transformation. The critique hinges on the nature of disjunctive propositions: in classical Islamic logic, a disjunctive like 'كل أ إما ب و إما ج' can be analyzed as a compound of two categoricals, 'كل أ هو ب وليس ج' or similar, but the author here claims they are directly categorical. The passage guards against a misunderstanding: that disjunctives are fundamentally different from categoricals, whereas the author insists on their identity.
