The Sheikh's Error: Deriving a Categorical from Two Disjunctives
Critique of the Sheikh's attempt to derive a categorical from two disjunctive premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why treating two exclusive disjunctions as categorical premises in the second figure leads to an invalid syllogism, and explain the mistake in Ibn Sina's attempted derivation.

Core Idea
Exclusive disjunctions are not categorical propositions — treating them as such violates the rules of syllogistic figures.

In this passage, the author critiques Ibn Sina's attempt to derive a categorical conclusion from two disjunctive premises using the second figure. He points out that although the disjunctives look like categoricals, they are fundamentally different. Understanding this error is crucial for correctly handling disjunctive propositions in logical reasoning, especially in Islamic legal and theological arguments where disjunctions appear frequently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
The Sheikh claims: 'Every A is either B or C' and 'Nothing of D is either B or C' yields 'Nothing of A is D'. Locate these in the passage and note the logical form: two disjunctives and a categorical conclusion.
2
Recognize the disjunctive nature
The premises are exclusive disjunctions (qadiyya munfasila), meaning 'A is either B or C but not both'. They are not simple categorical propositions of the form 'All A is (B or C)' as a single class.
The disjunction does not pick out a unitary predicate; it presents two mutually exclusive alternatives.
3
Check the attempted figure
The Sheikh uses the second figure (al-shakl al-thānī). In a valid second-figure categorical syllogism, both premises must be categorical, with the middle term as predicate in both. Here, the middle term would be 'either B or C', but it is not a single term.
Second figure conditions require both premises to be universal or particular affirmative/negative, not disjunctive.
4
Explain why the derivation fails
Even if we treat the disjunctives as categoricals, the form would be Camestres (AEE in the second figure). However, the disjunction 'either B or C' cannot serve as a unified middle term; it is a compound of two distinct possibilities. Hence the inference is invalid.
The error is a category mistake: treating a disjunctive proposition as if it were a simple categorical.
5
Conclude the logical lesson
The author asserts that the two disjunctives resemble categoricals but are not identical. The proper syllogistic treatment of disjunctives requires disjunctive syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i), not categorical figures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استنتاج الشيخ حملية من منفصلتين] و استنتج الشيخ من الشكل الثاني حملية كقولنا: «كل أ إما ب و إما ج» و «لا شيء من د إما ب و إما ج» ينتج «لا شيء من أ د». خطأ الشيخ في استنتاج الحملية من المنفصلتين] و أنت تعلم كون المنفصلتين شبيبتين بالحمليتين بل بالحقيقة هما هما.
[Deriving a categorical from two disjunctives] The Sheikh derived from the second figure a categorical, like our saying: 'Every A is either B or C' and 'Nothing of D is either B or C' yields 'Nothing of A is D'. The Sheikh's error in deriving a categorical from two disjunctives. And you know that the two disjunctives resemble the two categoricals, nay in reality they are the same.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage critiques the Sheikh's method of deriving a categorical syllogism from two disjunctive premises. The author states that the Sheikh attempted to use the second figure of categorical syllogism, giving an example: 'Every A is either B or C' and 'Nothing of D is either B or C' supposedly yielding 'Nothing of A is D'. The author declares this an error, because the two disjunctives are not genuinely categorical; although they resemble categoricals, in reality they are not identical. The error lies in treating exclusive disjunctions as if they were simple affirmative propositions, which misapplies the rules of categorical syllogistic. For instance, in fiqh, 'Every intoxicant is either wine or date-nabidh' and 'Nothing of the beverage in this cup is wine or date-nabidh' does not guarantee 'Nothing in this cup is an intoxicant' if the disjunction is not exclusive.

Key Takeaway
Disjunctive propositions, though structurally similar to categoricals, require different syllogistic treatment; treating them as categoricals in the second figure is a logical error.

Third Division: Categorical-Conditional Syllogisms – First Two Methods
Classification and first two methods of mixed categorical-conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four methods of mixed categorical-conditional syllogisms and construct the conclusion for the first two methods.

Core Idea
A conditional can combine with a categorical through a shared term to yield a new conditional.

This passage introduces the third division of conditional syllogisms: those composed of a categorical (ḥamliyya) and a conditional (muttaṣila) premise. The shared term (al-mushārik) can be either the antecedent or the consequent of the conditional, and the categorical can be placed as either the major or minor premise, giving four methods. The author details the first two methods, where the shared term is the consequent, and explains how to derive a new conditional conclusion. Understanding this allows you to chain categorical and conditional reasoning in Islamic sciences, such as extending known rulings to new cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared term (al-mushārik)
In a mixed syllogism, the categorical and conditional premises share one term. For the first two methods, this shared term is specifically the consequent (tālī) of the conditional.
The other possibility – shared term as antecedent – will be covered in the next two methods.
2
Determine the categorical's position
The categorical premise can be either the major (kubrā) or minor (sughrā) premise. In method one, it is major; in method two, it is minor. The position affects the final conclusion.
The categorical's own subject and predicate are irrelevant; only its relation to the shared term matters.
3
Construct the resulting conditional
The conclusion is always a conditional proposition. Its antecedent is exactly the antecedent of the original conditional. Its consequent is obtained by composing the consequent of the conditional with the categorical premise according to standard categorical syllogistic rules (as if the consequent were joined with the categorical).
This composition essentially performs a categorical syllogism where one premise is the categorical, and the other is the consequent treated as a term.
4
Apply the position condition
In method one (categorical = major), the categorical must function as the major premise in the composed categorical syllogism. In method two (categorical = minor), it must be minor. This ensures the valid direction of inference.
The condition guarantees that the linking of terms produces a sound result.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثالث: ما يتركب من الحملية و المتصلة و فيه أربعة مسالك: لأن المشارك للحملية: إما تالي المتصلة أو مقدمها. و كيف كان فالحملية: إما صغرى أو كبرى. و تنعقد الأشكال الأربعة في كل منها بحسب المتشاركين. [المسلكان الأول و الثاني] ما يكون المشارك تالي المتصلة: الأول: أن يكون المشارك تالي المتصلة و الحملية كبرى. و الثاني: أن يكون الحملية صغرى. [نتيجة هذين المسلكين] و هما ينتجان متصلة: مقدمها مقدم المتصلة و تاليها نتيجة التأليف بين التالي و الحملية. بشرط أن يراعى فيهما كون الحملية كبرى في الأول و صغرى في الثاني.
The third division: what is composed of a categorical and a conditional. There are four methods therein: because the shared term with the categorical is either the consequent of the conditional or its antecedent. And in any case, the categorical is either minor or major. And the four figures occur in each according to the shared terms. [The first and second methods] What is such that the shared term is the consequent of the conditional: First: that the shared term is the consequent of the conditional and the categorical is major. Second: that the categorical is minor. [Result of these two methods] And both produce a conditional: its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of composing the consequent and the categorical. Provided that in them the categorical is considered major in the first and minor in the second.
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage defines the third division of mixed conditional syllogisms: those combining a categorical (hamliyya) and a conditional (muttasila) premise. The author explains there are four methods (masālik) because the term shared between the two premises (المشارك) can be either the antecedent (muqaddam) or consequent (tālī) of the conditional, and the categorical can be either the major (kubrā) or minor (sughrā) premise. Within each case, all four figures of categorical syllogism can be formed depending on the overlapping terms. The passage then details the first two methods, where the shared term is the consequent of the conditional. In method one, the categorical is the major premise; in method two, the categorical is the minor premise. Both yield a conditional conclusion: its antecedent is the antecedent of the original conditional, and its consequent is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical premise according to categorical rules. The condition is that the categorical must be placed correctly (major in first, minor in second). For example, in fiqh: 'If it is date-nabidh, then it is an intoxicant' (conditional) and 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (categorical, major) yields 'If it is date-nabidh, then it is forbidden' (conditional). This shows how the sharing term (intoxicant) links the premises.

Key Terms
المتصلة (al-muttaṣila): conditional proposition
الحملية (al-ḥamliyya): categorical proposition
المشارك (al-mushārik): shared term
تالي (tālī): consequent
مقدم (muqaddam): antecedent

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A conditional with antecedent = premise's consequent and consequent = composition.
C) A conditional with antecedent = antecedent of the original conditional and consequent = composition of consequent and categorical.

Key Takeaway
Mixed categorical-conditional syllogisms produce a conditional conclusion by linking the antecedent of the conditional with the categorical via the shared term.

Two Paths of Mixed Hypothetical-Categorical Syllogisms
Author describes two forms of mixed hypothetical-categorical syllogisms and their conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid mixed hypothetical-categorical syllogism when the shared term is the consequent of the hypothetical, and identify the form of the conclusion.

Core Idea
A mixed syllogism with a shared consequent yields a conditional conclusion whose consequent is derived from a categorical syllogism.

After studying purely categorical and purely hypothetical syllogisms, the author now introduces mixed forms that combine both types. The key variable is which term is shared between the two premises. In the first two paths, the shared term is the consequent of the hypothetical, and the categorical can serve as either the major or minor premise. Understanding these paths equips you to handle arguments that begin with a conditional and then apply a categorical statement—common in kalam proofs and usul al-fiqh reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared term
In these two paths, the shared term (al-mushārik) is the consequent (tālī) of the hypothetical premise. For example, in 'If A then B' and 'B is C', the shared term is B.
Make sure you locate the consequent in the hypothetical, not the antecedent.
2
Determine the role of the categorical premise
In the first path, the categorical is the major premise (contains the predicate of the conclusion). In the second path, the categorical is the minor premise (contains the subject of the conclusion).
Check whether the categorical's subject/predicate line up with the shared term accordingly.
3
Combine the categorical with the consequent
Treat the consequent of the hypothetical as one premise and the categorical as another. Check if they form a valid categorical syllogism (e.g., Barbara). The middle term is whatever term appears in both the consequent and the categorical.
The figures and moods of categorical syllogisms apply here exactly.
4
Form the conclusion
The overall conclusion is a new hypothetical: its antecedent is the antecedent of the original hypothetical, and its consequent is the conclusion of the categorical combination from step 3.
If the original hypothetical is negative, the consequent of the conclusion changes accordingly.
5
Apply the conditions
The original hypothetical must be affirmative for the simplest case. If it is negative, then the combination yields a different consequent (as per the text). Also, the categorical combination itself must be valid (productive) in the appropriate figure.
These conditions ensure the deduction is sound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

٢. أو مقدمها.

The third section: What is composed of the categorical and the hypothetical. It has four paths: because the shared term with the categorical is either the consequent of the hypothetical or its antecedent. However the categorical is either minor or major. The four figures occur in each according to the shared terms. [The first and second paths: What has the shared term as the consequent of the hypothetical] First: that the shared term is the consequent of the hypothetical and the categorical is major. Second: that the categorical is minor. [Result of these two paths] They both produce a hypothetical: its antecedent is the antecedent of the hypothetical, and its consequent is the conclusion of the combination between the consequent and the categorical, provided that consideration is given to the categorical being major in the first and minor in the second. [Conditions for their production] It is required for their production: 1. The hypothetical is affirmative, and that the shared terms include a productive combination, observing the mentioned consideration. 2. Or if the hypothetical is negative, then the conclusion of the combination with the categorical is the consequent of the negative. [Examples of these two paths] Example of the first figure in the first path: It produces: 1. From the affirmative: (a) 'If every A is B, then every J is D' and 'Every D is H' [produces] 'If every A is B, then every J is H'. Because if the antecedent is assumed, the consequent together with the categorical are true. And whenever they are both true, the conclusion follows. So upon assuming the antecedent, the truth of the conclusion follows, which is what is sought. And analogously for the remaining moods of the hypothetical universal and particular in the two paths.
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage defines the third division of mixed syllogisms, specifically those composed of a categorical (qadiyya hamliyya) and a hypothetical conditional (qadiyya mutasila). The author explains that there are four possible paths based on two variables: (1) whether the term shared between the two premises is the consequent or the antecedent of the hypothetical, and (2) whether the categorical premise serves as the minor or major premise. However, the focus here is on the two paths where the shared term is the consequent of the hypothetical. In the first path, the categorical is the major premise; in the second, the categorical is the minor premise. Both paths yield a hypothetical conclusion whose antecedent is the antecedent of the original hypothetical, and whose consequent is the conclusion derived from combining the consequent of the hypothetical with the categorical premise. The author then states the conditions for valid production: the hypothetical must be affirmative, and the combination of the shared terms must itself constitute a valid syllogism (i.e., the consequent and the categorical must produce a valid result). If the hypothetical is negative, then the result of the combination becomes the consequent of the negative. An example is given from the first figure, first path: from 'If every A is B, then every J is D' and 'Every D is H', we deduce 'If every A is B, then every J is H'. The reasoning is that if we assume the antecedent 'Every A is B', the consequent 'Every J is D' follows, and together with the categorical 'Every D is H' we obtain 'Every J is H', which is then the consequent of the conditional conclusion. The author notes that this pattern applies to all moods of the hypothetical, both universal and particular, in both paths.

The Syllogism

Conditions for Mixed Conditional-Categorical Syllogisms
Conditions for mixed conditional-categorical syllogisms with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the two conditions for producing a valid mixed syllogism from a conditional major and a categorical minor in the first figure, and distinguish necessary from accidental conditionals.

Core Idea
A mixed conditional-categorical syllogism is valid only if the conditional is necessary and the shared terms satisfy the first figure.

This passage sets out the exact conditions under which a mixed syllogism—combining a conditional (if-then) premise with a categorical (predicative) premise—yields a valid conclusion. You will learn two key conditions and see them applied to affirmative and negative examples in the first figure. The passage also warns against accidental conditionals, which can produce false conclusions even when the form appears correct.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two conditions
The author states two conditions: (1) the conditional must be affirmative, and the two shared terms must be arranged in a productive combination; (2) the result of combining the minor with the consequent must be productive, especially when the conditional is negative.
The 'productive combination' refers to a valid categorical syllogism in the first figure between the consequent and the minor.
2
Analyze the affirmative example
Given: 'If every A is B, then every J is D' (major) and 'Every D is H' (minor). The conclusion is 'If every A is B, then every J is H'. The proof: assume the antecedent; then the consequent and the minor are both true; from them the conclusion follows.
This matches the first figure of categorical syllogisms (Barbara) within the consequent.
3
Analyze the negative example
Given: 'It is never the case that if A is B then not every J is D' (major) and 'Every D is H' (minor). The conclusion is 'It is never the case that if A is B then not every J is H'. The proof: assume the combination of the consequent's negation and the minor yields truth, which then implies the desired conditional.
The negative conditional is handled by assuming the truth of the combination of the consequent with the minor.
4
Identify the necessity condition
The author gives a counterexample: 'Whenever a human is rational, he is a horse' is an accidental conditional, not necessary. Even if the minor is true in a hypothetical world, the conclusion is false. Thus the conditional must be necessary (luzūmiyya) for validity.
Accidental conditionals (ittifāqiyya) lack a real connection between antecedent and consequent.

Conditions for the production of these two paths: 1. Affirmation of the conditional and inclusion of the two shared parts in a productive combination, observing the aforementioned observation. 2. Production of the result of the combination with the categorical, the consequent of the negative if the conditional is negative. Examples of these two paths: Example of the first figure in the first path: It produces: 1. Whenever they are true, the result follows. So upon the assumption of the antecedent, the truth of the result follows, which is desired. And from the negative: It produces: 'It is never the case that if A is B then not every J is D' and 'Every D is H' produces 'It is never the case that if A is B then not every J is H' because if the result of the combination is assumed true, namely 'Not every J is H' and 'Every D is H', and when they are true, 'Not every J is D' is true, which is the consequent. Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for producing valid mixed syllogisms that combine a conditional (mutasila) major premise with a categorical (hamliya) minor premise. The author states two conditions: first, the conditional must be affirmative, and the two shared terms (mustarakān) must be arranged in a productive combination; second, the result of combining the minor with the consequent of the conditional must be productive, especially when the conditional is negative. Then examples from the first figure are given for the first path. In the affirmative case: If every A is B, then every J is D; and every D is H; therefore, if every A is B, then every J is H. The proof: assume the antecedent; then the consequent and the minor are true; from them the conclusion follows. In the negative case: It is never the case that if A is B then not every J is D; and every D is H; therefore, it is never the case that if A is B then not every J is H. The proof: assume the combination of the consequent (not every J is D) and the minor (every D is H) yields the truth of 'not every J is H', which then implies the desired conclusion. The author then warns with a counterexample: 'Whenever a human is rational, he is a horse'—this conditional is accidental (ittifāqiyya), not necessary (luzūmiyya), and such syllogisms can be invalid unless the conditional is necessary. This shows that the condition of necessity must be observed.

Converting Negative Conditionals to Affirmative
Converting negative conditionals to affirmative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a negative necessary conditional into its affirmative equivalent and apply this conversion to simplify syllogisms with negative minor premises.

Core Idea
A negative conditional can be converted to affirmative, streamlining syllogistic reasoning.

Ibn Sina introduces an elegant shortcut in the Shifa: instead of dealing with negative conditionals directly, convert them to affirmative ones. The key equivalence is that 'It is never the case that if A then not B' is logically equivalent to 'If A then B'. This conversion allows any syllogism with a negative conditional premise to be handled using standard affirmative moods, eliminating the need for special negative rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negative conditional
Find the premise that is a negative necessary conditional, e.g., 'ليس كلما كان آب فليس ب' (It is not the case that whenever A is B then it is not J).
The negative must be a necessary (luzūmiyya) conditional.
2
Apply conversion (irtidād)
The negative conditional is converted to an affirmative by removing the outer negation and the inner negation: 'It is not the case that if P then not Q' becomes 'If P then Q'.
This conversion is truth-preserving under necessary connection.
3
Use the affirmative conditional
Now the premise is affirmative and can be used in standard syllogistic moods (e.g., first figure).
No special rules for negatives are needed after conversion.

The Sheikh's explanation of the production of these two paths: The Sheikh explained in the Shifa all the negative minor premises by another path based on the reduction of the negative necessary conditional to the affirmative, as is said in this example: If the minor is true, then it implies: 'Whenever A is B, then every J is D'.

Commentary
This passage introduces an alternative method by the Sheikh (Ibn Sina) for handling syllogisms with negative minor premises. Instead of directly dealing with the negative, one can convert the negative necessary conditional into its affirmative equivalent. The example given: if the minor (presumably a categorical) is true, then it implies an affirmative conditional. This method is more concise and builds on the idea that a negative conditional (e.g., 'It is not the case that if A then not B') is equivalent to an affirmative conditional ('If A then B'). This conversion allows the syllogism to proceed using the standard productive moods of the first figure without needing special rules for negatives.

Flaw in Ibn Sina's Conditional Conversion
Critique of Ibn Sina's method for converting negative conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw in Ibn Sina's conversion of negative conditionals to positive ones and evaluate the counterexample from physics.

Core Idea
Converting a negative conditional to positive fails when the categorical premise contradicts the antecedent.

Ibn Sina attempted a systematic method to produce conclusions from conditional syllogisms with negative minor premises. He proposed converting negative conditionals into positive ones. This passage reveals a critical flaw: such conversion assumes the categorical premise remains true when the antecedent holds, which is not always the case. A counterexample from natural philosophy—about void and dimensions—demonstrates the fallacy. Understanding this limitation is essential for rigorous syllogistic reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target method
Locate Ibn Sina's conversion: a negative conditional like 'Absolutely not if A then B' is treated as equivalent to 'Every time A then B'. This is the basis for producing syllogisms.
2
Understand the assumed validity
The method works only if the categorical premise (e.g., 'Every D is E') remains true even when the antecedent (A is B) is assumed true. Ibn Sina relies on this for the derivation.
3
Examine the counterexample
The passage gives: 'Every time a void exists, then some dimension is self-subsistent' is true, but also 'Nothing of the dimension is self-subsistent' is true in reality. The categorical premise contradicts the antecedent.
This shows the categorical premise is not true under the antecedent assumption.
4
Analyze the logical failure
If the categorical premise is false when the antecedent is true, the conversion leads to a false conclusion. The entailment from the minor premise is broken.
The method yields a false result if assumed together.
5
Assess the impact on syllogistic production
Ibn Sina's method cannot be universally applied. It requires the categorical premise to be compatible with the antecedent. This is a key constraint in conditional logic.

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of Ibn Sina's (the Shaykh) method for producing conclusions from conditional syllogisms with negative minor premises. The author first presents the results of some syllogistic combinations using variables (A, B, C, D, E). Then he summarizes Shaykh's method from al-Shifa' where he attempts to reduce a negative conditional to a positive one. The author then points out an error in this conversion, referencing his own earlier work on the entailment of conditionals. Finally, he presents a doubt raised by Shaykh himself: that the method only works if the categorical premise remains true under the assumption of the antecedent, which is not necessary. He gives an example from physics: 'Every time a void exists, then some dimension is self-subsistent' is true, but then the opposite categorical is also true in actuality ('Nothing of the dimension is self-subsistent'), which contradicts the antecedent. This shows the fallacy of the method. The passage follows the order: presentation of results, Shaykh's method, error, and doubt. It is a classic critical refinement where the author (likely a later commentator) identifies a flaw in the master's demonstration.

ارتداد السالبة إلى الموجبة
نقض التالي

Shaykh's Doubt and al-Khunaji's Objection on Conditional-Categorical Syllogisms
Dialectical exchange on conditional-categorical syllogism validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical exchange between Shaykh (Ibn Sina) and al-Khunaji regarding the validity of a conditional-categorical syllogism, and evaluate the logical moves of each party.

Core Idea
A syllogism's validity depends on truth persistence across contexts; al-Khunaji's exclusive disjunction resolves the doubt.

In this passage, we witness a sophisticated logical debate. The Shaykh (Ibn Sina) raises a doubt about the validity of a syllogism combining a conditional and a categorical premise. He then attempts to answer his own doubt using two strategies. But al-Khunaji, in his Kashf, objects to both answers and offers a deeper resolution involving an exclusive disjunction. Understanding this exchange sharpens your ability to evaluate arguments and counterarguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial valid inference
Locate where the author states a valid argument form. What are the premises and the conclusion? Note the logical relationship between the conditional and categorical premises.
The initial form is stated abstractly: from 'Every B is C' and a conditional, a conclusion follows.
2
Extract the Shaykh's doubt
Find the counterexample proposed by the Shaykh. What conditional and categorical premises does he use? Why do they lead to a false conclusion?
The counterexample involves 'vacuum' and 'dimension' to show that the categorical may not remain true under the assumption of the antecedent.
3
Analyze the Shaykh's two answers
Identify the two responses the Shaykh gives to his own doubt. First, he restricts the discourse; second, he argues that false premises can entail a false conclusion.
These answers aim to defend the validity of the syllogism under specific conditions.
4
Examine al-Khunaji's objections
For each of the Shaykh's answers, find al-Khunaji's specific objection. Why does he argue that the first answer is insufficient? Why is the second merely a case-specific refutation?
Al-Khunaji demands a proof for the first point and a general solution for the second.
5
Understand al-Khunaji's resolution
Al-Khunaji proposes an exclusive disjunction (maniat al-khuluw) between the negation of the antecedent and the result of the composition. Explain how this guarantees logical consistency when the categorical is true.
This resolution shows that the syllogism yields either the expected conclusion or the negation of the antecedent.

And regarding the second: it is a refutation of a specific counterexample, and from it does not follow the dropping of the objection raised against the principle of the syllogism. Rather, its answer is that from this syllogism there follows an exclusive disjunctive (man'at al-khalq) of the negation of the antecedent and the result of the composition: necessarily, reality is not empty of the negation of the antecedent and the syllogism. Because the categorical is true in reality, ... so if the consequent is true with it, the syllogism is true. And if it is not true, the negation of the antecedent is true due to the non-existence of the consequent.

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange concerning the validity of a certain type of conditional syllogism. The author first states a valid inference: from 'Every B is C' and some conditional? The text is unclear. Then a doubt (shakk) by the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) is introduced. The Shaykh argues that the conditional premises (qara'in) only produce a valid conclusion if the categorical (hamliyya) remains true when the conditional's antecedent is true. He provides a counterexample involving 'vacuum' and 'dimension' to show that this condition is not always met, leading to a false conclusion. The Shaykh then answers his own doubt: first, by restricting the discourse to cases where both premises are true together; second, by noting that the impossible can entail the impossible, so a false conclusion from false premises is acceptable. Then al-Khunaji (sahib al-Kashf) objects: the first answer fails because non-contradiction does not guarantee truth, and the second answer merely addresses a specific counterexample without refuting the general objection. Al-Khunaji proposes his own solution: from the syllogism follows an exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-khuluw) between the negation of the antecedent and the conclusion of the composition, meaning reality must contain either the negation or the syllogism's result. Since the categorical is true, if the consequent holds, the syllogism is valid; if not, the antecedent's negation holds, preserving logical consistency.

Objection and Refutation on Conditional Syllogisms
Author presents and analyzes a dialectical exchange on conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical exchange on a conditional syllogism, identifying the logical moves and evaluating the soundness of objections and responses.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism's validity depends on the context (qarina) and the truth of premises together.

In this passage, we witness a sophisticated dialectical exchange between Ibn Sina and al-Khunaji on the validity of a conditional syllogism. The argument revolves around whether a certain conditional syllogism is valid, with Ibn Sina providing an answer, al-Khunaji objecting, and the author defending Ibn Sina and refuting al-Khunaji. By working through this debate, you will sharpen your ability to evaluate logical arguments in Islamic dialectical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original conditional syllogism
Find the conditional proposition at the beginning: 'Whenever void exists, then some dimension is not a dimension.' This is the 'if-then' statement that forms the core of the syllogism under discussion.
This conditional is universal.
2
Extract Ibn Sina's answer to the doubt
Ibn Sina gives two answers: first, that the premises must be considered together under a specific context (qarina) where both are true; second, that if an impossible conclusion follows from premises, it does not invalidate them because impossibility can follow from impossibility.
His first answer restricts the scope of the syllogism.
3
Follow al-Khunaji's objection
Al-Khunaji raises two objections: (1) mere non-contradiction does not guarantee truth of premises, so a proof is needed; (2) refuting a specific counterexample does not defeat the general objection. He then proposes an alternative: from the syllogism follows a disjunctive proposition that covers all cases, making the conclusion necessary.
Al-Khunaji attempts to replace the conditional with a disjunctive.
4
Analyze the defense of Ibn Sina
The author defends Ibn Sina by clarifying that Ibn Sina's answer means the conclusion holds when premises are true under the assumption of the antecedent; thus, where premises are not true together, no counterexample applies. The first objection falls, and the second is universal.
The defense reinterprets Ibn Sina's words.
5
Critique al-Khunaji's alternative
The author refutes al-Khunaji's solution on three grounds: (1) the consequent cannot be true with the categorical, so the conclusion may not follow; (2) even if it could, the falsity of the consequent may be due to the categorical being false; (3) the conditional does not imply the disjunctive unless the disjunctive remains under the assumption of the antecedent.
Each ground systematically undermines al-Khunaji's logical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

«كلما كان الخلاء موجوداً فبعض البعد ليس ببعد».

اعتراض الخونجي على جواب الشيخ: و قال: ١ على الأول بأن عدم منافاته إياها لا يقتضي صدقها على تقدير صدقه، فلا بد من دليل؛ ٢. و على الثاني بأنه دفع نقض معين، و لا يلزم منه سقوط المنع الوارد على أصل القياس. بل جوابه أنه تلزم من هذا القياس منفصلة مانعة الخلق من نقيض المقدم و نتيجة التأليف: ضرورة عدم خلو الواقع عن نقيض المقدم و القياس. لأن الحملية صادقة في نفس الأمر، فلو صدق معها التالي، صدق القياس. وإن لم يصدق صدق نقيض المقدم لانتفاء التالي.
ثم إن شئنا نردّ هذه المنفصلة إلى المتصلة المذكورة أو نجعل هذه المنفصلة نفس المطلوب.

Chapter Two: On Syllogism – Section Two: On the Divisions of Syllogism, First Division: Conjunctive – On Conditional Syllogisms.
'Whenever void exists, then some dimension is not a dimension.'
The Shaykh's answer to his doubt: He answered: First, regarding the first, that we specify the speech by the context in which both are true together. And regarding the second, that the necessary from the two premises is true, and it is possible that the impossible implies the impossible.
Al-Khunaji's objection to the Shaykh's answer: He said: First, regarding the first, that its not contradicting them does not entail their truth upon the assumption of its truth, so a proof is needed. Second, regarding the second, that it is a refutation of a specific counterexample, and it does not entail the fall of the objection directed at the original syllogism. Rather, his answer is that from this syllogism follows a disjunctive that prevents the absence of the contradictory of the premise and the result of the composition: necessarily, reality is not free from the contradictory of the premise and the syllogism. Because the categorical is true in itself; so if the consequent is true with it, the syllogism is true. And if it is not true, the contradictory of the premise is true due to the negation of the consequent.
Then, if we wish, we reduce this disjunctive to the aforementioned conditional, or we make this disjunctive itself the desired conclusion.
Defense of the Shaykh's answer: Know that this is unsound speech: Because the meaning of the Shaykh's speech regarding the first is that he said: 'We say: If the minor premise is true with the major premise on the assumption of the antecedent, the conclusion follows from them; so where it is not true, a counterexample does not apply to us.' And thus the first objection falls. Similarly the second, because the answer is universal in all instances of this objection.
Refutation of al-Khunaji's answer: As for the answer, it is apparently false: First, because the truth of the consequent with the categorical is impossible, so it is possible that the conclusion does not follow. Second, if that is granted, we do not grant that if the consequent is not true with the categorical, the antecedent is necessarily false, because it is possible that the lack of truth is due to the absence of the categorical at that time. Third, if that is granted, we do not grant the implication of the conditional for the disjunctive. Indeed, that would follow only if the disjunctive remained upon the assumption of the truth of the antecedent. The truth is contrary to this because if we assume the truth of the antecedent, the elimination between the two parts follows. Because then the categorical becomes false, so neither the contradictory nor the conclusion is true.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage is a detailed dialectical exchange about the validity of a conditional syllogism. The example given is a universal conditional: 'Whenever void exists, then some dimension is not a dimension.' The Shaykh (Ibn Sina) first answers a doubt about such syllogisms by specifying that the premises are considered together under a certain context (qarina), and that if the impossible follows from premises, that does not invalidate them. Al-Khunaji objects to both parts: (1) mere non-contradiction does not guarantee truth, and (2) responding to a specific counterexample does not refute a general objection. He then proposes that from the syllogism a disjunctive follows that necessarily covers all cases. The author defends the Shaykh, arguing that the Shaykh's answer means that the conclusion holds when the premises are true under the assumption of the antecedent, so no counterexample applies. He then refutes al-Khunaji's proposed answer on three grounds: first, the truth of the consequent with the categorical is impossible, so the conclusion need not follow; second, even if it were possible, the falsity of the consequent could be due to the categorical being false; third, the conditional does not necessarily imply the disjunctive unless the disjunctive is considered under the assumption of the antecedent. This is a sophisticated discussion on the conditions for valid inference in conditional syllogisms, specifically those involving compound premises.

The Third and Fourth Methods in Mixed Conditional-Categorical Syllogisms
Author defines two new methods for mixed conditional-categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid conclusion from a mixed conditional-categorical syllogism by correctly identifying the placement of the categorical premise (minor or major) and applying the rule of compositional production.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, the placement of the categorical premise determines whether you get a direct conclusion or need to universalize the antecedent.

This passage introduces two additional methods for analyzing syllogisms that combine a conditional premise with a categorical premise. These methods are crucial for deriving rulings in Islamic jurisprudence where a conditional rule is applied to a specific case. We will learn how to identify the shared term (the antecedent of the conditional) and how the categorical premise's position affects the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared term (المشارك)
Find the term that appears in both premises; in these methods, it is the antecedent of the conditional (muqaddam al-muttasila). This term is the link between the two premises.
2
Determine the placement of the categorical premise
In the third method, the categorical is the minor premise (sughrā). In the fourth method, it is the major premise (kubrā). The placement dictates how the composition is formed.
The text states: 'المسلك الثالث: ما يكون المشارك مقدم المتصلة و الحملية صغرى. و الرابع: ما يكون الحملية كبرى.'
3
Form the composition (التأليف)
Combine the categorical premise with the antecedent of the conditional to produce a new antecedent for the conclusion. This composition must observe the placement of the categorical as specified.
4
Check production conditions
Ensure the composition is productive. The production may be immediate or may require making a particular antecedent of a universal conditional into a universal. This step ensures the syllogism yields a valid conclusion.
The rule from the scholars: 'المتشاركان إن اشتملا على تأليف منتج ... إما ابتداء أو بعد جعل المقدم الجزئي للمتصلة الكلية كلية.'
5
Formulate the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional proposition. Its antecedent is the result of the composition, and its consequent is the consequent of the original conditional. Ensure that the placement of the categorical has been respected in the antecedent.
The conclusion is a conditional, not a categorical, allowing further reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثالث: ما يكون المشارك مقدم المتصلة و الحملية صغرى. و الرابع: ما يكون الحملية كبرى. و النتيجة فيها متصلة مقدمها نتيجة التأليف مرعياً فيه وضع الحملية كما مر. و تاليها تالي المتصلة. و الشيخ طول في الشفاء بتفصيل ضروب هذين المسلكين و ما ذكر ضابطاً شاملاً، و كنا صاحب الكشف. و قد وجدنا من كلام بعض أفاضل الزمان أدام الله أيامهم ضابطاً حسناً، فنُبينه مع ما زاد عليه زويتنا. فنقول: المتشاركان إن اشتملا على تأليف منتج، بشرط رعاية وضع الحملية كما ذكر، إنتاجها: إما ابتداء أو بعد جعل المقدم الجزئي للمتصلة الكلية كلية.
The third method: what has the shared term as the antecedent of the conditional, and the categorical is the minor premise. The fourth: what has the categorical as the major premise. The conclusion in them is a conditional, its antecedent is the result of the composition, with observance of the placement of the categorical as mentioned, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional. The Shaykh elaborated in al-Shifa' detailing the moods of these two methods, but did not mention a comprehensive rule. We found a good rule from the speech of some eminent contemporaries, may God prolong their days, so we will explain it along with what we added from our own view. We say: the two shared terms, if they contain a productive composition, with the condition of maintaining the placement of the categorical as mentioned, their production is either directly or after making the particular antecedent of the universal conditional into a universal.
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage presents two additional methods (maslakān) for analyzing mixed syllogisms that combine a conditional (muttasila) premise with a categorical (hamliyya) premise. The shared term (al-mushtarak) is the antecedent of the conditional. In the third method, the categorical premise is the minor premise (sughrā); in the fourth, the categorical is the major premise (kubrā). The conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent is the result of combining the categorical with the antecedent of the original conditional, and whose consequent is the consequent of the original conditional. The author notes that Avicenna (al-Shaykh) detailed the moods of these methods in al-Shifā' but did not give a comprehensive rule. Then the author introduces a rule from some contemporary scholars (perhaps Sirāj al-Dīn al-Urmawī) and adds his own contribution. The rule states: for two conditional premises to yield a product, their placement must observe the categorical premise as described. The production may be immediate or after quantifying the antecedent of the conditional universally (i.e., converting a particular antecedent to a universal one). This is similar to how in categorical syllogisms, one may need to convert a premise to make the syllogism valid. For example, in fiqh: 'If a transaction involves gharar, it is prohibited; This transaction involves gharar' – the categorical minor confirms the antecedent, and the conclusion (this transaction is prohibited) follows directly. The fourth method would have the categorical as the major: 'If a transaction involves gharar, it is prohibited; Every transaction involving gharar is invalid' – here the categorical major is an additional rule, and the conclusion combines the two conditionals.

Third and Fourth Methods of Mixed Syllogisms
Defines two methods of mixed syllogisms with conditional and categorical premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of mixed syllogisms to construct a conditional conclusion from a conditional and a categorical premise in the third or fourth method, and distinguish between direct and indirect composition.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms combine a conditional and a categorical to yield a new conditional via the antecedent.

In this lesson, we explore how to combine a conditional premise (mutasila) with a categorical premise (hamliyya) to produce a new conditional. The third method places the categorical as the minor premise; the fourth as the major. The key is to check whether the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional form a productive categorical syllogism; if not, a contrapositive step is needed. This method, detailed by Siraj al-Din al-Urmawi, extends the work of Ibn Sina in al-Shifa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional and its parts
Locate the conditional premise (e.g., 'If every B is A, then Z'). Its antecedent is 'every B is A', its consequent is 'Z'. Note the terms (B, A).
The antecedent is a universal categorical proposition.
2
Identify the categorical premise and its terms
The categorical premise is a simple subject-predicate statement (e.g., 'Every J is B'). Identify its subject (J) and predicate (B).
3
Determine the method
If the categorical is the minor premise (subject of the categorical appears in the antecedent), it is the third method. If the categorical is the major premise (predicate appears in the antecedent), it is the fourth.
In the example, 'Every J is B' is minor, so it is the third method.
4
Check direct productivity
See if the categorical and the antecedent can form a valid categorical syllogism (first figure conditions). Here, 'Every J is B' and 'Every B is A' yield 'Every J is A' via Barbara. This is the result of composition.
If productive, the conclusion's antecedent is this result. If not, proceed to step 5.
5
Apply the rule for non-productive cases
If the premises do not directly compose, use the categorical together with the result of composition (or its universal contrapositive) as a new premise to generate the antecedent of the conditional.
In such cases, the syllogism may be from the third figure. The conditional must be universal.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage defines the third and fourth methods of mixed syllogisms (also called 'conjunctive conditional syllogisms'), where one premise is a conditional (mutasila) and the other is a categorical (hamliyya). The third method has the categorical as the minor premise, the fourth as the major. The conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent is the result of combining the categorical with the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the consequent of the original conditional. The author (likely al-Isfahani) notes that Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) in his Shifa detailed the moods but did not provide a comprehensive rule. He then presents a rule from Siraj al-Din al-Urmawi: the two premises are either directly productive (if the categorical and the conditional yield a conclusion immediately, possibly after making a particular antecedent universal) or not. If not, one must use the categorical together with the result of the composition (i.e., the conclusion from another syllogism) or its contrapositive to produce the antecedent of the conditional. The conditional must be universal in this section because it becomes the major premise in the first figure. The proof shows that when the product is the direct result, the syllogism is from the first figure; when it is the contrapositive, from the third. An example is given: "Every J is B, and whenever (every B is A) then Z" yields "It might be that when (every J is A) then Z." This illustrates the first mood of the first figure in the third method.

Producing Universal Conclusions from Shared Premises
Conditions for universal conclusion from shared premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conditions under which a categorical premise and a universal conditional premise produce a universal conclusion, and validate the proof using either direct composition or reductio.

Core Idea
Two premises yield a universal conclusion only when the conditional is universal and the middle term aligns.

This passage expands on Ibn Sina's analysis of compound syllogisms, focusing on when a categorical premise and a conditional premise together produce a universal conclusion. You will learn to recognize the necessary conditions and follow two proof strategies: one via the third figure and one via reductio ad absurdum. The author also critiques a previous claim about Ibn Sina's comprehensiveness, refining the interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the categorical premise (حملیة) and the conditional premise (متصلة). The conditional must be universal ('كلية') to serve as the major in the first figure.
Check the text: 'و المتصلة يجب كونها في هذا القسم كلية'.
2
Determine the middle term
The middle term is the antecedent of the conditional. It must be the same term that appears in the categorical premise to form a valid first-figure categorical syllogism (the 'result of composition').
In the example, the antecedent is 'كل ج ب' and the categorical is 'كل ج ب'? Actually careful: example uses variables ج, ب, أ.
3
Apply the direct proof (third figure)
Assume the antecedent of the conditional is true together with the categorical. Then the result of composition is true. Since the antecedent implies the consequent, the consequent follows. This yields the conclusion when the categorical and the antecedent are both true.
The proof: 'كلما فرض مقدّم المتصلة يصدق هو مع الحملية. و كلما صدقا معاً صدقت نتيجة التأليف. فكلما صدق المقدم صدقت نتيجة التأليف. لكن كلما صدق المقدم صدق تاليه'.
4
Apply the reductio proof
Assume the conclusion is false (the universal conditional is not true). Then the negation of the consequent leads to a contradiction via a first-figure categorical syllogism. The contradiction shows the original assumption is false, so the conclusion is true.
The text: 'إذا لم تصدق النتيجة... ليس البتة إذا كان (كل ج 1) فوز. فنضته مع الكبرى لينتج من الثاني: ليس البتة إذا كان (كل ب أ) فأكل ج 1. و ذلك باطل'.
5
Evaluate the author's critique
The author argues that Ibn Sina did not clarify all moods that produce universal conclusions using these proofs; rather, Ibn Sina used a method similar to the rule of hypothesis in modern logic. Recognize that the earlier claim was an oversimplification.
The text: 'لكن ادعاء المصنف هاها خطأ لأن الشيخ ما بين الضروب التي نتائجها كلية بأحد البرهانين: بل اتخذ مسلكاً آخر شبيهاً بقاعدة الفرض في المنطق الرياضي'.

Commentary
This passage is a complex discussion on the conditions under which a categorical premise and a conditional premise (specifically, a universal conditional) produce a universal conclusion in a syllogism. The author is likely commenting on or expanding Ibn Sina's teachings from al-Shifā'. The passage begins by stating that the syllogism produces a particular conclusion from a universal premise in certain configurations. Then it outlines two proof methods: one using the third figure (with the middle term as the antecedent of the conditional) and one using reductio ad absurdum from the second figure. The key point is that the conditional must be universal to serve as the major premise in the first figure. The author gives an example of a productive mood from the first figure's first course with two shared premises. Then it provides two more detailed proofs: the first from the third figure showing that if the antecedent is true together with the categorical, then the result of composition is true, and since the antecedent implies the consequent, the desired conclusion follows. The second proof is by reductio: assuming the negation, it leads to a contradiction that is shown to be false by a categorical syllogism of the first figure. The passage then references Ibn Sina's al-Shifā' and critiques an earlier claim by the author (likely the original author of Qustas) that the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) clarified all moods with universal conclusions by either proof; the commenter argues that Ibn Sina actually used a different approach similar to the rule of hypothesis in modern mathematical logic. Finally, it explains that 'al-mushtarikān productive' means the two shared premises are productive, like adding a description to the described, for example 'the dog's cowardice' meaning cowardice from both sides. The passage thus serves as a critical refinement of a previous interpretation.

Two Proofs for Conditional Syllogisms
Author presents two proofs (burhan) to establish productive conditional syllogisms with shared parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the direct and contradiction proofs for a conditional syllogism with a shared term.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms are validated either by direct composition or by contradiction.

This lesson unpacks two methods—direct and by contradiction—that classical logicians use to prove the validity of a conditional syllogism when the conditional and categorical premises share a term. These proofs are essential for understanding how compound arguments work in Islamic logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of the syllogism
Note the conditional premise (e.g., 'If A then B') and the categorical premise (e.g., 'Every C is A'). The shared term 'A' links them. The author uses variables ج, ب, أ for terms.
2
Follow the direct proof (from the third figure)
Assume the antecedent of the conditional is true along with the categorical. Then the 'composition result' (natijat al-ta'lif) is true. Since the antecedent implies the consequent, the composition result implies the consequent. So if the composition result holds, the consequent holds.
This is a conditional conclusion.
3
Follow the proof by contradiction (khalf)
Assume the desired conclusion is false. This yields the negation of the conditional premise. Combine that negation with the categorical premise using a second-figure syllogism to derive a contradiction, proving the original conclusion must be true.
The contradiction shows the assumption is impossible.
4
Recognize the reliance on shared terms
Both proofs require that the conditional and categorical share a term. Without that, the syllogism would not be productive. The author notes that Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) demonstrated this for all productive moods with shared parts in al-Shifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage presents two proofs (burhan) for the validity of conditional syllogisms that are composed of a conditional premise and a categorical premise sharing a term. The first proof uses the third figure (presumably of conditional? Actually it says 'from the third' but here it is a direct implication: if the antecedent of the conditional is true together with the categorical, then the composition result is true, and since the antecedent implies the consequent, the composition result implies the consequent. The second proof is by contradiction (khalf): assume the conclusion is false, then the negation of the conditional is true, which together with the major yields a contradiction via the second figure, showing the original claim holds. The author attributes this demonstration to Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) in his work al-Shifa, claiming it covers all productive moods with shared parts. Note: The terms 'ج', 'ب', 'أ' are variable placeholders. The word 'فوز' appears to be a term variable (perhaps 'Z') but its exact reading is uncertain due to OCR.

Critique of Conditional Syllogism Proofs
Author critiques the claimed proofs, asserting second and third figures are not productive in these moods, and gives counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the shared parts in a conditional syllogism fail to produce the intended conclusion and explain the author's critique of Ibn Sina's proofs.

Core Idea
Shared parts do not always directly yield the conclusion; extra inference or conversion may be needed.

This passage critiques two proofs from Ibn Sina's logical works concerning conditional syllogisms. The author argues that in the second and third figures, the shared parts (terms common to the conditional and categorical premises) do not always produce the expected conclusion directly. Two examples are given: one where the conclusion follows only after a composition inference, another where the product is the converse of the expected conclusion. Understanding this critique is crucial for evaluating the completeness of Ibn Sina's syllogistic system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the critique
The author is critiquing two proofs that claim the second and third figures of categorical syllogisms are productive when combined with conditional premises. The critique asserts these figures are not known to be productive in certain moods.
The proofs themselves are not quoted; we infer them from the critique.
2
Locate the shared parts in the first example
The example uses 'every J is B' (categorical) and 'if no J is A then Z' (conditional). The shared part is the term 'J', which appears in both antecedents.
Shared parts (al-mushtarakan) are terms common to both premises.
3
Trace the inference to the composition result
The author shows that from the premises, one cannot directly derive 'if some B is not A then Z'. Instead, a composition inference is needed: combine 'every J is B' with the conditional's antecedent 'no J is A' to deduce 'some B is not A', then link to Z.
The composition result (natijat al-ta'lif) is an intermediate step.
4
Examine the second example
The second example uses 'no J is B' as the categorical. Here the product is the converse of the expected conclusion, meaning the conclusion's terms are reversed.
Conversion ('aks al-natija) indicates the conclusion is not in the expected order.
5
Understand the qualification
The author admits that if the results are universal, Ibn Sina's proofs are complete. The critique only stands for particular conclusions.
This shows the critique is nuanced, not a wholesale rejection.

Identifying Unproductive Syllogisms in the Second and Third Figures
Author critiques two proofs in second and third figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a second or third figure categorical syllogism fails to produce a valid conclusion and apply the same critique to analogous arguments in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Not every syllogism in the second or third figure yields a certain conclusion—some modes are inherently unproductive.

In previous lessons, we learned about categorical syllogisms and the conditions for validity in the first figure. However, not all figures are equally reliable. The author of our text now challenges two specific proofs that rely on the second and third figures, showing that their conclusions are not guaranteed. Let us examine his critique and learn how to identify such unproductive arguments, a skill essential for rigorous reasoning in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proofs under scrutiny
The author mentions two proofs (برهانان) in the second and third figures. Look at the symbolic structures: in the first example, the premises are 'Every C is B' and 'No C is A'. In the second, 'No C is B' and 'Every A is B'. These are typical second and third figure forms.
ج = middle term, ب = major term, أ = minor term
2
Extract the expected conclusion vs. the actual product
For the first proof, the author says the product is not the direct conclusion but the result of composition (نتيجة التأليف). For the second, the product is the converse of the result (عكس النتيجة). This indicates that the expected conclusion does not follow directly.
The actual derived propositions are different from what a naive logician would assume.
3
Analyze the author's demonstration of unproductivity
The author uses conditional reasoning: 'Whenever (no C is A) then some B is not A' and shows that this does not guarantee the inverse. In the second example, he combines the premises with the converse of the conclusion to produce a new syllogism, revealing that the original conclusion is not entailed.
The author's method involves showing that the premises can be true while the alleged conclusion is false.
4
Generalize the critique to other moods
The author instructs 'قياس على ذلك سائر الضروب' (measure other moods by this). This means we can apply the same logical test to any syllogism to check if the conclusion truly follows.
This is a call to develop a habit of verifying syllogistic validity rather than assuming it.

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of two specific proofs (برهانان) in the context of categorical syllogisms. The author begins by stating that he has doubt about these proofs because he has previously established that the second and third figures, in these two particular modes, do not yield known conclusions (i.e., their productivity is uncertain). He then instructs to measure other moods in every figure accordingly.

The author provides two examples. In the first example, he demonstrates a case where the two shared terms (المتشاركان) do not produce a conclusion, but the product of the composition (نتيجة التأليف) is something else. He uses symbolic letters: ج (middle term), ب (major term), أ (minor term). The argument is presented as a series of conditional statements and conversions. The second example illustrates a case where the product is the converse of the result (عكس النتيجة). Here, the author shows a syllogism in the second figure where the conclusion obtained is the converse of what would be expected.

The commentary requires careful tracking of the logical symbols. The author uses 'كل ج ب' (Every C is B) and 'لا شيء من ج أ' (No C is A) etc. The structure involves checking when 'لا شيء من ج أ' holds, then applying known rules to derive 'بعض ب ليس أ' (Some B is not A) and then converting. The second example is more complex: from 'لا شيء من ج ب' and 'كل أ ب' (Every A is B), which is the converse of the original conclusion, the author constructs a new syllogism by combining with the major premise.

In both cases, the author concludes 'و هو المطلوب' (that is what was required), indicating he has demonstrated the point. He ends by generalizing to other moods and invoking divine knowledge.

Fourth Division: Compound of Categorical and Disjunctive
Clarifies the fourth division of compound syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve the ambiguity in the phrase 'النتيجة من كل تأليف حملية واحدة' and correctly construct the fourth division of compound syllogisms using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Each pairing of a categorical with a disjunct yields a categorical conclusion, not one overall categorical.

The fourth division of compound syllogisms involves mixing a categorical premise with a disjunctive premise. The original statement 'the result from each composition is a single categorical' can be misinterpreted. A closer look reveals that it means each combination of a categorical with a disjunct gives a categorical conclusion, not that the whole syllogism has only one categorical conclusion. Understanding this is crucial for analyzing compound arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure
Recognize that this compound syllogism has a disjunctive premise (with multiple disjuncts) and an equal number of categorical premises, one for each disjunct. Each categorical premise pairs with a disjunct to form a sub-argument.
2
Parse the ambiguous phrase
The phrase 'النتيجة من كل تأليف حملية واحدة' can be read as 'the result from each composition is a single categorical' or 'the result from every composition is a single categorical'. Note that 'كل تأليف' could mean each composition or every composition, and 'حملية واحدة' could mean one categorical or a single categorical.
3
Apply the commentator's correction
The commentator clarifies that the emphasis is on the result being categorical in nature (not disjunctive), not on it being numerically one. Also, 'every composition' refers to each pairing of a categorical with a disjunct, not the entire syllogism. Therefore, multiple categorical conclusions are produced.
4
Construct an Islamic example
For instance: Disjunction: 'The prayer is either fajr or zuhr.' Categoricals: 'Fajr is recited aloud' and 'Zuhr is recited silently.' Then the first pairing gives 'Fajr is recited aloud' and the second gives 'Zuhr is recited silently.' Both conclusions are categorical, not disjunctive.
Use a fiqh or usul example to practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و الحمليات فيه لا تخلو من
القسم الرابع
ما يتركب من الحملية و المنفصلة
ا أن تكون بعدد أجزاء الانفصال و النتيجة من كل تأليف حملية واحدة

And the categoricals in it are not free from the fourth division: what is composed of the categorical and the disjunctive, that they be equal to the number of parts of the disjunction, and the result from each composition is a single categorical. 'The result from each composition is a single categorical': the expression is initially unclear in meaning, and the apparent (interpretation) is that the result is categorical, not disjunctive, not that it is one and not many, for the oneness or multiplicity of the result is not of such importance here. And also it is probable that the intended meaning is that if the categoricals are many and equal to the number of parts of the disjunction, then each categorical of those categoricals and each part of the parts of that disjunction combine and produce a categorical. If this was the intended meaning, then the word 'composition' in the phrase 'every composition' does not refer to the syllogism.

Commentary
This passage introduces and clarifies the fourth division of compound syllogisms (القسم الرابع), which consists of syllogisms composed of a categorical premise (حملية) and a disjunctive premise (منفصلة). The author first states the rule: the categorical premises should be equal in number to the parts of the disjunction, and the result from each composition (تأليف) is a single categorical proposition.

Then a commentary note (likely from the author or a later commentator) addresses an ambiguity in the phrase 'النتيجة من كل تأليف حملية واحدة' (the result from each composition is a single categorical). The initial reading might suggest that the result is one categorical, not many. However, the commentator clarifies that the intended meaning is that the result is categorical in nature (not disjunctive), not that it is numerically one. The oneness or multiplicity is not important here. Instead, the likely meaning is that when there are multiple categoricals (equal to the number of disjuncts), each categorical combines with each part of the disjunction to produce a categorical conclusion. In that case, the word 'composition' (تأليف) in 'each composition' does not refer to the entire syllogism, but to each pairing of a categorical and a disjunct.

This passage is an expansion (probe) of a compressed definition, explaining the precise meaning of a technical statement.

The Categorical-Disjunctive Mixed Syllogism: Divided Syllogism and Complete Induction
Defines the fourth category of mixed syllogisms and illustrates with an example from the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a mixed categorical-disjunctive syllogism and verify that its conclusion follows from the premises.

Core Idea
A mixed categorical-disjunctive syllogism enumerates all cases and shows each yields the same categorical conclusion.

In this lesson, we explore the fourth category of mixed syllogisms, which combines a disjunctive major premise with multiple categorical minor premises. This logical tool, known as the 'divided syllogism' or 'complete induction,' allows us to reason exhaustively about all possibilities. By studying its structure and an example from the first figure, you will learn how to construct and validate such arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive major premise
In the example, the major premise is 'Always, either every A is B or every A is C'. Note the number of disjuncts.
2
Count the categorical minor premises
There are two categorical minors: 'Every B is D' and 'Every C is D'. This matches the number of disjuncts.
3
Pair each disjunct with a minor premise
Combine 'every A is B' with 'every B is D' to form a categorical syllogism. Similarly, combine 'every A is C' with 'every C is D'.
4
Check that both pairs yield the same conclusion
Both yield 'every A is D'. This is the hallmark of a valid divided syllogism.
5
Recognize the alternative names
This structure is called 'al-qiyas al-muqassam' (divided syllogism) and 'al-istiqra' al-tamm' (complete induction).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الرابع ما يتركب من الحملية و المنفصلة. ا أن تكون بعدد أجزاء الانفصال و النتيجة من كل تأليف حملية واحدة. فهذا مسلكان و هو المستى بن القياس المقسم و يقال له الاستقراء التام أيضاً. المركب من الحملية و المنفصلة؛ بل إلى القياسات المركبة من أجزاء ذلك القياس. و لكن النظر في مثال المصنف يبدي أنه يقصد أن نتائج التأليفات من كلّ مقدّمة حملية و مشاركيا في المنفصلة تكون واحدة. فانظر إلى مثال الضرب الأول من الشكل الأول: دائما إما كل آب أو كل آج و كل ب ده و كلج ده ينتج کل آد.
The fourth section: what is composed of categorical and disjunctive. Either it is [such] that [the categorical premises] are equal to the number of the parts of the disjunction, and the conclusion from each composition is a single categorical. So these are two approaches, and it is called the divided syllogism, and it is also called complete induction. It is composed of categorical and disjunctive; rather, [it extends] to syllogisms composed of parts of that syllogism. But examination of the author's example shows that he intends that the results of the compositions from each categorical premise and its counterpart in the disjunctive are one [and the same]. So look at the example of the first mood of the first figure: Always, either every A is B or every A is C; and every B is D; and every C is D; therefore every A is D.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage is a definition and illustration of the fourth category of mixed syllogisms, which combine categorical and disjunctive premises. The passage first defines the structure: the number of categorical premises equals the number of parts in the disjunction (i.e., for each disjunct there is a corresponding categorical premise), and the conclusion of each pairing is a single categorical proposition. It then notes two names for this type: 'divided syllogism' (al-qiyas al-muqassam) and 'complete induction' (al-istiqra' al-tamm). The author explains that the syllogism is composed of categorical and disjunctive, but it extends to compositions of parts of that syllogism. To clarify, the passage points to an example from the first mood of the first figure: 'Always, either every A is B or every A is C; and every B is D; and every C is D; therefore every A is D.' Here, the disjunctive major premise has two disjuncts: 'every A is B' and 'every A is C'. The categorical minors are 'every B is D' and 'every C is D'. The conclusion 'every A is D' is obtained from both pairings. This illustrates that the conclusion of each composition (major's disjunct with corresponding minor) is the same categorical proposition, which is also the final conclusion of the whole syllogism.

Key Terms
القياس المقسم
الاستقراء التام
المنفصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example from the passage (first mood, first figure): 'Always, either every A is B or every A is C; and every B is D; and every C is D; therefore every A is D.' Which of the following best describes why this syllogism is called 'complete induction'?
A) Because it uses a disjunctive major premise.
B) Because it enumerates all possible cases (A is B or A is C) and shows each leads to the same conclusion.
C) Because it has three premises instead of two.
D) Because the conclusion is a universal affirmative.

Key Takeaway
The divided syllogism, or complete induction, is a mixed categorical-disjunctive syllogism that achieves certainty by exhaustively covering all disjuncts and verifying that each leads to the same categorical conclusion.

Ensure the conclusion is identical from both pairings.
