The Compound Syllogism: Structure and Conditions
Defines the compound syllogism and its subdivisions with conditions for the middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a compound syllogism and verify the conditions on the middle term and figures.

Core Idea
A disjunctive premise combined with categoricals yields a certain categorical conclusion.

In this passage, the author defines a powerful logical tool: the compound syllogism (al-qiyas al-muqassam), also called complete induction. It blends a disjunctive premise with categorical premises to produce a firm conclusion. Understanding its structure and the conditions on the middle term is essential for constructing rigorous proofs in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, especially when enumerating all possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Locate the disjunctive (munfasila) premise in the passage. In the example, it is 'Always either every A is B or every A is C'. Note the two disjuncts (al-qismayn).
The disjunction must be exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw) for the induction to be complete.
2
Identify the categorical premises paired with each disjunct
For each disjunct, there is a categorical premise that combines to yield the conclusion. Here: 'every D is B' pairs with 'every A is B', and 'every C is D' pairs with 'every A is C'.
Each combination forms a simple categorical syllogism.
3
Determine the figure and middle term placement
Check if the compositions are from one figure or different figures. In the example, both are from the first figure. The middle terms are the terms connecting the premises: in the first combination, the middle term is B; in the second, it is C.
The author explains that when all compositions are from one figure, each has a different middle term; otherwise, the middle term may be unified.
4
Verify the conclusion follows from each combination
Confirm that 'every A is D' follows from both disjuncts via the respective categorical premises. This shows the compound syllogism is valid: the conclusion is the same regardless of which disjunct is true.
The validity relies on the disjunction covering all possibilities and each sub-argument being valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الأول: المركب من الحملية و المنفصلة، و يقال له «الاستقراء التام» أيضاً. و لكن النظر في مثال المصنف يبدي أنه يقصد أن نتائج التأليفات من كلّ مقدّمة حملية و مشاركيا في المنفصلة تكون واحدة. فانظر إلى مثال الضرب الأول من الشكل الأول: دائما إما كل آب أو كل آج، و كل بده، و كلج ده، ينتج كل آد.
أقسام القياس المقسم: و يتألف فيه كل حملية مع جزء من أجزاء الانفصال قياساً منتجاً للحملية المطلوبة: إما من شكل واحد، أو من أشكال مختلفة. و حينئذ يلزم أن تكون أجزاء الانفصال مشتركة في طرف من النتيجة و الحمليات في طرف آخر.
وحدة الأوسط و تعدّده في القياس المقسم: و الأوسط في كل قياس غيره في الآخر - إن كانت التأليفات من شكل واحد - و إلا اتحدت قضيتان بطرفيهما من الحمليات و من أجزاء الانفصال. و إن لم يكن من شكل واحد جاز اتحاد الوسط.
وضع الأوساط في الشكلين الأول و الرابع: فالمنفصلة إن كانت صغرى، كانت الأوساط: محمولات أجزائها و موضوعات الحمليات في الشكل الأول.
The first approach: the composite of categorical and conditional, and it is also called 'complete induction'. But considering the author's example, it appears that he intends that the results of the compositions from each categorical premise and its counterpart in the conditional are one. So look at the example of the first mood of the first figure: Always 'either every A is B or every A is C', and 'every D is B', and 'every C is D', produces 'every A is D'.
Divisions of the compound syllogism: In it, each categorical premise together with a part of the parts of the disjunction composes a syllogism producing the required categorical conclusion: either from one figure, or from different figures. Then it is necessary that the parts of the disjunction share in one extreme of the conclusion and the categorical premises in the other extreme.
Unity and multiplicity of the middle term in the compound syllogism: The middle term in each syllogism is other than that in the other - if the compositions are from one figure - and otherwise two propositions become united by their two extremes from the categorical premises and from the parts of the disjunction. And if they are not from one figure, the unification of the middle term is permissible.
Placement of the middle terms in the first and fourth figures: If the disjunctive is the minor, then the middle terms are: predicates of its parts and subjects of the categorical premises in the first figure.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the compound syllogism (al-qiyas al-muqassam), also called complete induction (al-istiqra' al-tamm). The author begins by stating that this approach combines categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (munfasila) premises. He then provides an example from the first figure: 'Always either every A is B or every A is C, and every D is B, and every C is D, therefore every A is D.' This shows that the conclusion follows from either disjunct combined with the relevant categorical. The author then divides the compound syllogism into two types: those where all compositions are from a single figure, and those from different figures. He notes that in the first case, the parts of the disjunction share one extreme of the conclusion, and the categorical premises share the other extreme. Regarding the middle term, when all compositions are from one figure, each syllogism has a different middle term; otherwise, two propositions may become united by their extremes. Finally, he explains the placement of middle terms in the first and fourth figures: if the disjunctive premise is the minor, then in the first figure the middle terms are the predicates of its parts and the subjects of the categorical premises. Throughout, the author uses the logical variables (A, B, C, D) to illustrate the structure, which is typical in classical logic texts.

Key Terms
القياس المقسم (al-qiyas al-muqassam): A compound syllogism composed of one categorical and one disjunctive premise, arranged so that each part of the disjunction combines with the categorical to produce a categorical conclusion. [Note: This term is used in post-Avicennan logic; earlier logicians like al-Farabi referred to it as complete induction.]
الاستقراء التام (al-istiqra' al-tamm): Complete induction, which is a form of compound syllogism that enumerates all disjuncts and concludes a universal categorical. [Note: In earlier texts, istiqra' often meant simple enumeration; here it is identified with the compound syllogism.]
الأوسط (al-awsat): The middle term, which in a compound syllogism may be multiple if the compositions are from one figure, or unified if from different figures. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable across the tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The compound syllogism (complete induction) yields a certain universal conclusion when the disjunction covers all possibilities and each sub-syllogism is valid.

Conditions for the Divided Syllogism (al-Qiyas al-Muqassam)
Author states conditions for a productive divided syllogism and gives an example from the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions for a valid divided syllogism and verify its conclusion using a disjunctive inference.

Core Idea
A divided syllogism requires a true affirmative disjunction and proper term placement.

This lesson presents the conditions for a productive divided syllogism, a key disjunctive argument form. You will learn the three conditions and see how they work in a first-figure example using Arabic variables. Understanding these conditions helps you validate real arguments in Islamic legal reasoning, such as when jurists reason from two possible categories to a common conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction and categorical premises
Locate the disjunctive proposition (e.g., 'إما كل أ ب أو كل أ ج') and the accompanying categorical propositions (e.g., 'كل ب د' and 'كل ج د'). Note their positions as major or minor premises.
In the example, the disjunction is minor, and the categoricals are major.
2
Check condition 1: Truth of 'man' al-khalq' (at least one disjunct true)
Ensure that the disjunction is true in the sense that it excludes the possibility that ALL disjuncts are false. That is, at least one disjunct is true. This is called 'صدق منع الخلق'.
If the disjunction were false or exclusive in a different way, the argument may not be productive.
3
Check condition 2: The disjunction is affirmative
The disjunction must be affirmative (موجبة), not negative. In Arabic, the disjunction 'إما... أو...' is affirmative. If it were negative (e.g., 'ليس إما... أو...'), the conditions would not be met.
4
Check condition 3: Shared terms form a productive composition
In the first figure, the middle terms must be such that each disjunct, when combined with the corresponding categorical, yields the same conclusion. In the example, the middle terms are B and C, both leading to D in the categoricals, so the conclusion 'كل أ د' follows.
The author notes that in the second figure the predicates are shared, and in the third figure the subjects are shared.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و بالعكس في الرابع. وضع الأوساط في الشكلين الثاني والثالث .۱ محمولات أجزاء الانفصال و الحمليات جميعاً في الثاني. .. وموضوعاتها في الثالث سواء كانت المنفصلة صغرى أو كبرى. شرائط إنتاج القياس المقسم: ۱. صدق منع الخلق على المنفصلة ٢. وكون المنفصلة موجبة و اشتمال المتشاركين في كل شكل على تأليف منتج. مثال الضرب الأول من الشكل الأول: دائماً إما كل أ ب أو كل أ ج و كل ب د و كل ج د ينتج كل أ د. برهانه إنه لا بد من صدق أحد أجزاء الانفصال. فيصدق مع الحملية المشاركة له و ينتج المطلوب.
And conversely in the fourth. Placing the middles in the second and third figures: 1. The predicates of the parts of the disjunction and all the categoricals [are] in the second, and their subjects in the third, whether the disjunction is minor or major. Conditions for production of the divided syllogism: 1. Truth of prevention of non-existence on the disjunction, 2. The disjunction is affirmative, and the shared [propositions] in every figure contain a productive composition. Example of the first form of the first figure: Always either every A is B or every A is C, and every B is D, and every C is D, produces every A is D. Its proof: It is necessary that one of the parts of the disjunction is true; thus it is true with the categorical that participates with it and produces the required.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage provides the conditions for a productive divided syllogism (القياس المقسم), which is a disjunctive syllogism where the disjunction may be either the major or minor premise. It begins by describing the placement of terms in the second and third figures: in the second figure, the predicates of the disjuncts and all categoricals are the same; in the third figure, their subjects are the same, regardless of whether the disjunction is minor or major. Then it lists three conditions for production: (1) the disjunction must be true with respect to preventing non-existence (i.e., it is a true inclusive disjunction where at least one disjunct is true), (2) the disjunction must be affirmative (not negative), and (3) the shared terms in each figure must form a productive combination. The example given is from the first figure: 'Always either every A is B or every A is C, and every B is D, and every C is D, therefore every A is D.' This is a valid syllogism because if the disjunction is true, then either every A is B or every A is C; in either case, the corresponding categorical premise (every B is D or every C is D) applies, so the conclusion every A is D follows. The proof notes that since one disjunct must be true, it combines with the relevant categorical to yield the conclusion.

Key Terms
القياس المقسم
المنفصلة
الحمليات
منع الخلق

Why Exclusive Disjunction (Mani'at al-Jam') Does Not Produce
Author explains why exclusive disjunction yields no conclusion unless it transforms into inclusive disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why exclusive disjunction (Mani'at al-Jam') does not yield a conclusion in a disjunctive syllogism, and identify the exception when it transforms into an inclusive disjunction.

Core Idea
Exclusive disjunction fails because both disjuncts can be false; only inclusive disjunction is directly productive.

In this passage, the author explains a crucial limitation of exclusive disjunction in logical reasoning. While disjunctive syllogisms are common in Islamic legal reasoning, not all disjunctions produce valid conclusions. Understanding when a disjunction yields a conclusion is essential for sound argumentation in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of disjunction
Recall that exclusive disjunction (Mani'at al-Jam') means exactly one option is true, while inclusive disjunction (Mani'at al-Khuluw) means at least one is true. This distinction is critical for evaluating syllogisms.
2
Identify why exclusive disjunction fails
The author states that exclusive disjunction does not produce because it is possible that all its parts are false. That is, if you deny one part, you cannot conclude the other because both could be false.
3
Recognize the exception
The passage notes that if the parts of an exclusive disjunction are the contradictories of what would be in an inclusive disjunction, then the conclusion follows. Essentially, when the disjuncts are contradictories, the exclusive disjunction behaves like an inclusive one.
The exception effectively transforms exclusive into inclusive.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider: 'Either this liquid is intoxicating or it is not permissible to drink.' If it is exclusive, denying it is intoxicating does not allow concluding it is permissible to drink because it might be something else impure. But if the options are contradictories (like 'it is intoxicating' and 'it is not intoxicating'), then it is actually an inclusive disjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عدم إنتاج مانعة الجمع. و لا تنتج مانعة الجمع لجواز خلق أجزائها جميعاً، إلا إذا كانت أجزاؤها نقائض ما يجب أن تكون في مانعة الخلق، فتلزمها النتيجة لانقلاعها إلى المانعة الخلق المنتجة بالذات.
Non-production of exclusive disjunction. And exclusive disjunction does not produce because it is possible that all its parts are false, unless its parts are contradictories of what must be in the inclusive disjunction; then the result follows because it transforms into the inclusive disjunction which produces essentially.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage addresses the non-productivity of 'Mani'at al-Jamʿ' (exclusive disjunction), where exactly one of the disjuncts is true and the other false. The author states that such a disjunction does not lead to a conclusion because it is possible that all its parts are false (i.e., neither disjunct is true). This is in contrast to 'Mani'at al-Khuluw' (inclusive disjunction), where at least one disjunct is true. However, there is an exception: if the parts of the exclusive disjunction are the contradictories of what would be the parts of an inclusive disjunction, then the conclusion does follow because the exclusive disjunction effectively transforms into an inclusive one, which by its nature yields a conclusion. In other words, when the exclusive disjunction is expressed as a pair of contradictory statements, it behaves like an inclusive disjunction because one of the contradictories must be true.

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because it is a categorical proposition

Key Takeaway
Exclusive disjunction (Mani'at al-Jam') is not productive in disjunctive syllogisms because both disjuncts could be false; only when it transforms into an inclusive disjunction does the conclusion follow.

Al-Maraghi's Objection: Consistency of Premises in Divided Syllogism
Al-Maraghi objects that categoricals may not remain true together using conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Maraghi's objection by applying contrapositive and simple conversion to check the simultaneous truth of categorical premises in a divided syllogism.

Core Idea
A divided syllogism's premises must be simultaneously true; conversion can reveal they are not.

The divided syllogism (qiyas muqassam) requires that the categorical premises remain true together. Sharaf al-Din al-Maraghi objects that this condition is not automatically guaranteed. In this lesson, we will walk through his demonstration: given a disjunction and two categoricals, applying logical conversions produces contradictory statements, showing the premises cannot both be true. Understanding this objection sharpens your ability to evaluate the validity of disjunctive arguments.

Guided Path
1
State the condition for a valid divided syllogism
The divided syllogism requires that the categorical premises (e.g., 'every B is C' and 'every not-B is C') are both true at the same time. Al-Maraghi challenges this.
2
Identify the disjunction and categoricals
The disjunction is 'always either every A is B or every A is not B'. The categoricals are 'every B is C' and 'every not-B is C'.
These are the premises for a divided syllogism about A and C.
3
Apply contrapositive conversion to the first categorical
Convert 'every B is C' by contraposition (ʿaks al-naqīḍ): 'every not-C is not B'. This is a valid immediate inference.
Contrapositive preserves truth: if all B are C, then all non-C are non-B.
4
Apply simple conversion to the result
Convert 'every not-C is not B' by simple conversion (ʿaks al-mustawī): 'some not-B is not C'. This is also valid (universal affirmative becomes particular affirmative).
Simple conversion of 'every X is Y' yields 'some Y is X'.
5
Compare with the second categorical
The second categorical is 'every not-B is C'. But the derived statement says 'some not-B is not C'. These are contradictory: one says all not-B are C, the other says some not-B are not C. Therefore both original categoricals cannot be true together.
This contradiction shows the condition for a productive divided syllogism fails in this case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: إنّما ينتج القياس المقسم إن لو بقيت الحمليات صادقة معاً و ذلك غير واجب لأنه إذا صدق دائماً إما كل أ ب أو كل أ ليس ب و كل ب ج و كل ما ليس ب ج ينعكس عكس نقيض كلّ منهما بعكس المستوي إلى منافي الأخرى.
For it is said: The divided syllogism only produces if the categoricals remain true together, and that is not necessary because if it is always true that either every A is B or every A is not B, and every B is C, and every not-B is C, then each of them by contrapositive conversion converts by simple conversion to the opposite of the other.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection of Sharaf al-Din al-Maraghi against the divided syllogism. The objector argues that the condition for a productive divided syllogism — that the categorical premises remain true simultaneously — is not guaranteed. To demonstrate, he considers a specific case: suppose we have a disjunction 'always either every A is B or every A is not B', and two categoricals: 'every B is C' and 'every not-B is C'. In this situation, if we apply contrapositive conversion (عكس نقيض) followed by simple conversion (عكس المستوي) to each categorical, we obtain statements that contradict one another. Specifically, 'every B is C' converts by contrapositive to 'every not-C is not B', then by simple conversion to 'some not-B is not C', which conflicts with 'every not-B is C'. Similarly, the other categorical converts to something that contradicts the first. Thus, the two categoricals cannot both be true, violating the required condition. Therefore, the divided syllogism may not produce a conclusion.

Key Terms
عكس المستوي
عكس نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Contrapositive conversion then simple conversion
C) Simple conversion then contrapositive conversion
D) Obversion then conversion

Key Takeaway
Al-Maraghi demonstrates that the categorical premises in a divided syllogism may not be simultaneously true, using immediate inferences (conversions) to derive a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Al-Maraghi's Objection on the Divided Syllogism
Al-Maraghi objects that if the minor term is existent or non-existent, one of the categoricals becomes false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw that al-Maraghi exposes in the divided syllogism when the middle term is existent or non-existent.

Core Idea
The middle term's ontological status determines whether the categorical premises can all be true.

In constructing a divided syllogism, the logician assumes that the two categorical premises 'Every B is C' and 'Every not-B is C' are both true. Al-Maraghi challenges this assumption by examining the nature of the middle term C. He shows that whether C is existent or non-existent, one of the premises becomes false. This objection deepens our understanding of the conditions required for a valid syllogism and has implications for arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Recall the structure of the divided syllogism
The divided syllogism uses two universal affirmatives: 'Every B is C' and 'Every not-B is C'. The conclusion is that everything (whether B or not-B) is C. Identify these premises in the passage.
Note that 'C' is the middle term, 'B' is the major term, and the conclusion is about all things.
2
First case: C is an existent entity
Consider C as an existent thing (وجودي). The premise 'Every not-B is C' would require that all non-B are existent. But non-B includes non-existents (معدومات) and composites (مجتمعات) that are not actual individuals. Since an existent predicate cannot apply to non-existents, this premise is false.
Non-existents are not things that can have properties like existence.
3
Second case: C is a non-existent entity
Consider C as a non-existent thing (عدمي). Then it is the absence of something, say X. Since C is the non-existence of X, it cannot be truly affirmed of X. If X is B, then 'Every B is C' is false because B would be said to be non-existence of itself. If X is not B, then 'Every not-B is C' is false because it would attribute non-existence of X to something other than X, which is impossible.
The impossibility arises from the principle that a thing cannot be its own negation or a non-existence of something else.
4
Conclusion of the objection
In both cases, one of the categorical premises is false. Therefore, the condition of simultaneous truth is violated, and the divided syllogism does not produce a valid conclusion without further qualification.
This shows that the divided syllogism is not unconditionally valid; it depends on the ontological nature of the terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لأن ج إذا كان أمراً وجودياً، يمتنع صدقه على كل ما ليس ب الشامل للمعدومات و المجتمعات. و إن كان عدمياً، لا بد وأن يكون عدم أمر. فذلك الأمر: إن صدق عليه ب كذبت الحملية الأولى؛ و إلا كذبت الثانية، لامتناع حمل نقيض الشيء عليه.
And because if C is an existent thing, it is impossible that it is true of everything that is not B, which includes non-existents and composites. And if it is a non-existent, it must be the non-existence of something. So that thing: if B is true of it, the first categorical is false; otherwise the second is false, because it is impossible to attribute the opposite of a thing to it.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection of Sharaf al-Din al-Maraghi, which focuses on the nature of the term 'C' in the categorical premises. The objector argues that the categoricals 'every B is C' and 'every not-B is C' cannot both be true. Consider the case where C is an existent entity (وجودي). Then it cannot be truly affirmed of everything that is not B, because the domain of 'not-B' includes non-existents (معدومات) and composites (مجتمعات), which are not actual individuals. Since C is existent, it cannot apply to non-existent things. On the other hand, if C is a non-existent entity (عدمي), then it must be the non-existence of something (i.e., the absence of some thing). Let that thing be X. If X is B, then the categorical 'every B is C' is false because C is non-existence of B, so B cannot be C; if X is not B, then 'every not-B is C' is false, because it would require that non-B is non-existence of a non-B, which is impossible. Thus, regardless of whether C is existent or non-existent, one of the categoricals must be false. Therefore, the condition of simultaneous truth is violated, and the divided syllogism does not produce a conclusion.

Key Terms
وجودي (existent)
عدمي (non-existent)
حملية (categorical)

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because C is a universal

Key Takeaway
Al-Maraghi's objection demonstrates that the divided syllogism's condition of simultaneous truth hinges on the ontological status of the middle term, and that one premise will be false regardless of whether that term is existent or non-existent.

Distinguishing Validity from Soundness in Syllogisms
Author refutes an objection regarding truth of premises in divided syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between validity (luzūm al-natījah) and soundness (ṣidq al-muqaddimāt) and explain why an objection to soundness does not refute validity.

Core Idea
A syllogism can be valid even if its premises are false; validity is about implication, not truth.

The passage presents an objection claiming a divided syllogism is refuted because its premises are not true. The author refutes by clarifying that logical validity only requires that the conclusion follows from the premises, not that the premises are actually true. This distinction is foundational for understanding logical arguments in Islamic sciences like kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection presents a syllogism: 'Every existent is either necessary or not necessary; every necessary is possible; every not necessary is possible; therefore every existent is possible.' The objector claims this is refuted because the premises are false.
The objection confuses soundness (truth of premises) with validity (logical implication).
2
Locate the author's response
The author says: 'We only claim the necessity of the conclusion on the assumption of the truth of the premises. So if they are not true in some materials, it is no refutation.' This directly addresses the distinction.
3
Distinguish validity and soundness
Validity means: if the premises are true, the conclusion must be true. Soundness means: premises are actually true and the argument is valid. The objector attacks soundness, but validity remains intact.
The author uses the term 'luzūm al-natījah' (necessity of conclusion) for validity.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the argument: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; nabīdh is an intoxicant; so nabīdh is forbidden.' Even if one disputes the premise 'every intoxicant is forbidden', the syllogism itself is valid. The dispute is about soundness, not validity.

It should not be said: This is refuted by the truth of our statement: 'Every existent is either necessary or not necessary' and 'Every necessary is possible in the general sense' and 'Everything not necessary is possible in the general sense.' Because we say: We do not concede the truth of the second because: 1. What has necessary extremities is neither necessary nor possible in the general sense. 2. And the impossible that is possible in the general sense is only that which is necessarily non-existent. Its answer: Because 1. We only claim the necessity of the conclusion on the assumption of the truth of the premises. So if they are not true in some materials, it is no refutation against us. For the non-existence of the antecedent is not a refutation of the claim of implication. And the two corruptions only follow when the disjunctive has two contradictories. It appears from this answer that the author only accepts the objection in some divisions of the divided syllogism, as in this division: Always: either every A is B or every A is not B, and every B is D, and everything not B is D, so every A is D. B and not B in this syllogism are contradictories, and likewise the next division devoid of the disjunctive: Every B is D, and everything not B is D, so everything is D. Commentary
This passage presents an objection (naqd) to a divided syllogism, followed by a rebuttal and an explanatory comment. The objection claims that the premises are not all true, citing examples about necessity and possibility. The author refutes by stating that validity only requires that the conclusion follows from the premises, not that the premises are actually true in every material. He distinguishes between the truth of premises and the logical implication (luzum). The commentator then clarifies that the author restricts the objection to cases where the disjunctive premise has two contradictories (naqīḍān), as shown in the scheme: 'Either every A is B or every A is not B; every B is D; everything not B is D; therefore every A is D.' Here B and not B are contradictories. In the next division, the disjunctive is absent, but the same conclusion follows. The commentator notes that the famous logician al-Maraghī's proof applies without a disjunctive, so the author ultimately rejects the objection for the absolute divided syllogism. The lesson uses the Islamic example of existence divided into necessary and contingent, with possibility in the general sense (al-imkān al-‘āmm).

Structure of the Divided Syllogism with Contradictory Disjuncts
Author refutes an objection regarding truth of premises in divided syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a divided syllogism where the disjunctive premise contains two contradictories and verify that the conclusion follows necessarily.

Core Idea
A divided syllogism with contradictory disjuncts is self-validating: the conclusion follows directly from the two conditionals.

The commentator clarifies that the author only accepts the objection when the disjunctive premise has two contradictories (naqīḍān). He gives a precise scheme: 'Either every A is B or every A is not B; every B is D; everything not B is D; therefore every A is D.' This form is robust and valid regardless of the material truth of the premises. Understanding this structure helps in analyzing logical arguments in kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Ensure the disjunction covers all possibilities and the two disjuncts are contradictories (e.g., B and not B). In the scheme, 'every A is B' and 'every A is not B' are contradictory.
Literally, the disjuncts are 'every A is B' and 'every A is not B'. They cannot both be true and one must be true.
2
Extract the conditional premises
There are two conditionals: if every A is B, then every A is D (implied by 'every B is D'), and if every A is not B, then every A is D (implied by 'everything not B is D').
The conditionals are categorical statements: 'every B is D' and 'everything not B is D'.
3
Verify the conclusion
Since one of the disjuncts must be true, and each leads to D, the conclusion 'every A is D' follows necessarily. This is a classic proof by cases.
4
Compare with the next division
The commentator mentions another division without a disjunctive premise: 'Every B is D and everything not B is D, so everything is D.' This is even stronger: it does not rely on a disjunction, but the conclusion covers all items (not just A).
Al-Maraghī's proof applies here without a disjunctive, showing the objection fails for absolute divided syllogism.

Compound Syllogisms with Disjunctive and Categorical Premises
Classification of compound syllogisms with disjunctive and categorical premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct compound syllogisms that yield a disjunctive conclusion (māni‘at al-khuluw) when categorical premises equal or exceed the number of disjuncts.

Core Idea
When categoricals match disjuncts, the conclusion is a disjunction, not a single categorical.

So far we have studied syllogisms where the conclusion is a single categorical proposition. But in compound syllogisms that combine a disjunctive major with categorical minors, the result may itself be a disjunction. This passage classifies such cases based on whether the number of categorical premises equals or exceeds the number of disjuncts. Understanding this structure is essential for evaluating complex arguments in Islamic legal reasoning, where a ruling may be presented as a disjunction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive major premise
Find the disjunctive proposition (e.g., 'Every action is either obligatory or supererogatory'). Note the number of disjuncts. In the example, there are two: 'obligatory' and 'supererogatory'.
The disjunction here is of the 'preventing emptiness' type (māni‘at al-khuluw), meaning at least one disjunct must be true.
2
Count the categorical premises
List the categorical premises that accompany the disjunctive major. In the example: 'Every obligatory action is rewarded' and 'Every supererogatory action is rewarded'. Here the number of categoricals (2) equals the number of disjuncts (2).
If the numbers differ, the conclusion type changes.
3
Check the pairing of categoricals with disjuncts
Each categorical premise should share a term with one disjunct. In the example, the first categorical pairs with 'obligatory' (both share 'obligatory action'), and the second pairs with 'supererogatory'. This pairing must be 'productive' (i.e., yield a valid categorical syllogism within the compound).
If one disjunct pairs with two categoricals (because categoricals exceed disjuncts), that is also valid but produces distinct results.
4
Determine the conclusion type
If the categoricals equal the disjuncts, the results from each pairing will be different categoricals. These are combined into a disjunction of the same type as the major (māni‘at al-khuluw). In the example, both results are 'rewarded' — they unite, so the conclusion collapses to a single categorical: 'Every action is rewarded.' If they had been different, the conclusion would be a disjunction.
When results are identical, they merge into one part of the disjunction.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage outlines a classification of compound syllogisms that combine categorical and disjunctive premises. The author distinguishes cases where the premises' results are not a single categorical statement, or where the number of categorical premises does not match the number of disjuncts in the disjunctive premise. Two types are given. Composing Categoricals with a Disjunctive to Yield an Exclusive Disjunction
Rules for composing categoricals with a disjunctive premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid composite syllogism that combines categorical premises with a disjunctive premise to produce an exclusive disjunction conclusion.

Core Idea
The art of composing categoricals with disjunctives yields exclusive disjunctions that map each condition to its consequence.

This lesson unpacks a key logical operation: how to take a disjunctive major premise and several categorical minor premises, then combine them to produce a new disjunctive conclusion. The passage from our source explains the rules for when the categoricals equal the number of disjuncts, exceed them, or fall short. Mastering this allows you to build compound arguments that are essential in theological and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive type
First, determine if the disjunctive premise is exclusive (مانعة الخلق) or non-exclusive (مانعة الجمع). The rules differ. Our passage focuses on exclusive disjunction.
The text later mentions that the discussion is for exclusive disjunctions; non-exclusive ones require separate treatment.
2
Count categoricals vs. disjuncts
Count the number of categorical premises and the number of parts (disjuncts) in the disjunctive premise. There are three subcases: equal, more, or fewer categoricals.
3
Apply the participation rule
For each categorical, check which disjunct it shares a term with (productive participation). The shared term allows combining the categorical with that disjunct to produce an intermediate result.
If two results are identical, they merge into one part of the final disjunction.
4
Form the conclusion as an exclusive disjunction
The final conclusion is an exclusive disjunction of the intermediate results. If any categorical shares two disjuncts, its result becomes a compound part. If a categorical shares only one disjunct, the other disjunct remains as is.
The author gives examples with variables: 'every A is B or every A is C, and every B is D, and every C is D, so every A is D.' Here one categorical (every A is B or C) combines with two categoricals to yield a single universal conclusion (every A is D) because the results coincide.

Source Text

فإما أن تكون الحمليات بعدد أجزاء الانفصال:

٢. و إن نقصت الحمليات، مثلاً كحملية مع منفصلة ذات جزئين، فإن شاركت الجزئين، أنتجت منفصلة مانعة الخلق من النتيجتين؛ و إن لم تشارك إلا جزءاً واحداً، أنتجت مانعة الخلق من نتيجة التأليف و الجزء الغير المشارك.

Chapter Two: On Syllogism - Section Two: On the Divisions of Syllogism - First Division: Conjunctive [Syllogism] - On Conditional Syllogisms. And the two categoricals are contraposed by contraposition. The second method of the author [consists] of categorical and disjunctive. And it is of two types: The first division: composed of the exclusive disjunction. Either the categoricals are equal in number to the parts of the disjunction: 1. Each one of them participates with one of the parts of the disjunction in a productive participation, but the results of the compositions are diverse, so it produces an exclusive disjunction from the results of the compositions. If two results coincide, we make them one part of the result. b. When the categoricals are not equal in number to the parts of the disjunction: 1. If the categoricals increase, necessarily a part of the disjunction shares two categoricals, if one categorical increases, otherwise the one categorical would not have entry into the syllogism. Always: Every A is B or every A is C, and every B is D, and every C is D, so every A is D. Two results: two results of S, 'Ayn, B. We made, we made B. And then it produces an exclusive disjunction, and a part of its parts is composed of two results from the results of the compositions due to the sharing of a part of the two categoricals. 2. If the categoricals are less, for example one categorical with a disjunctive having two parts: if it shares the two parts, it produces an exclusive disjunction from the two results; if it shares only one part, it produces an exclusive disjunction from the result of the composition and the non-shared part. And the Shaykh said: 'A single categorical when it is minor does not produce.' And you have known its falsity. All this is when the disjunctive is exclusive. As for when it is non-exclusive: The second division: composed of the non-exclusive disjunction. Al-Shifa' al-Qiyas, p. 353 line... And what the Shaykh claimed is limited to when the disjunctive has negative parts. And al-Khunaji objected to the Shaykh with this statement: The Shaykh claimed that a single categorical minor with a disjunctive major does not produce. And its falsity is apparent because if it is true: Every B is J, and it is not that: either no A is B or no H is B, [then] either no J is A or no J is [something]. (Kashf al-Asrar, p. 390, lines 6-8). And the editor (Khalid al-Rub) said in the margin: I did not come across this claim from the Shaykh; rather it is apparent that the Shaykh explicitly states the opposite, and he says in al-Shifa' (al-Qiyas, p. 353): Every B is J, and always every B is either H or... produces: every J is either H or... (al-Shifa', al-Qiyas, p. 353). And it seems that the editor did not know that al-Khunaji's statement is about disjunctions with negative parts, not about those with positive parts.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage is a technical exposition on how to combine categorical and disjunctive premises in a conjunctive syllogism to produce a conclusion that is itself a disjunctive proposition, specifically an exclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلق). The author first distinguishes two scenarios: when the number of categorical premises equals the number of parts of the disjunction, and when it does not. In the first scenario, each categorical shares with one disjunct in a productive way, but the results differ; the overall conclusion is an exclusive disjunction combining those results. If two results are identical, they are treated as one part. In the second scenario, if there are more categoricals than disjuncts, one disjunct must share with two categoricals, leading to a conclusion where a part is compound. If there are fewer categoricals, the author gives an example of one categorical with a two-part disjunction: if the categorical shares both parts, the conclusion is an exclusive disjunction of the two results; if it shares only one part, the conclusion is an exclusive disjunction of that result and the non-shared part. The author then critiques the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) for claiming that a single categorical minor with a disjunctive major does not produce a conclusion, calling it false. Finally, the passage notes that this discussion applies only to exclusive disjunctions; for non-exclusive disjunctions (مانعة الجمع), a different treatment is needed, and a marginal note clarifies that al-Khunaji's objection is specifically about disjunctions with negative parts.

The Syllogism

The Condition for a Valid Mixed Categorical-Disjunctive Syllogism (Māni'a al-Jam')
Condition for a valid compound syllogism with exclusive disjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a mixed categorical-disjunctive syllogism with an exclusive disjunction (māni'a al-jam') satisfies the condition for producing a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
Every result of composing the categorical with each disjunct must entail the shared term.

When a categorical premise is combined with an exclusive disjunctive premise (māni'a al-jam'), a valid conclusion does not follow automatically. The author lays down a precise condition: the shared term must be entailed by every composition. Let's unpack this condition and learn how to apply it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Locate the munfaṣila māni'a al-jam' in the argument. It will have two parts (disjuncts) that cannot both be true but may both be false.
This is an exclusive disjunction, not inclusive.
2
Identify the categorical premise
Find the ḥamliyya (categorical) proposition. It will share a term with each part of the disjunctive.
The shared term is called al-ṭaraf al-mushārik.
3
Check the composition results
For each part of the disjunctive, form a conditional syllogism with the categorical. Ask: does the resulting proposition (natījat al-ta'līf) entail the shared term?
If yes for every part, the condition is met.
4
Determine the conclusion form
If all parts share predicates, the conclusion is a new exclusive disjunction of the results. Otherwise, it is a disjunction of results and the non-shared term.
The author distinguishes two divisions based on sharing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فشرط إنتاجه: كون كل نتيجة من نتائج التأليفات مع الحملية المشاركة لها منتجة للطرف المشارك من المنفصلة. و حينئذ تلزم النتيجة مانعة الجمع: من نتائج التأليفات، إن كانت الأجزاء كلها مشاركة المحمليات، كما في القسم الأول، وإلا فمن نتائج التأليفات و الطرف الغير المشارك، كما في القسم الثاني. إن الطرف المشارك إذا كان لازماً لنتيجة التأليف بالقياس المؤلف من الحملي و المتصل، تلزم المنافاة بين نتائج التأليفات أو بين النتائج و الطرف الغير المشارك، إذ المنافي للازم يكون منافياً للملزوم. مثال الأول: ينتج: دائما إما بعض ب آ أو بعض ج د» مانعة الجمع، «كل ج ب » و «كل ه ج». دائما إنما كل ج ا أو كل ه ده مانعة الجمع
The condition for its production: that every result of the compositions along with the categorical that shares with it produces the shared term of the disjunctive. And then the conclusion follows as a disjunctive exclusive (māni'a al-jam'): from the results of the compositions, if all parts share the predicates, as in the first division; otherwise from the results of the compositions and the non-shared term, as in the second division. Indeed, if the shared term is a necessary consequence of the result of the composition, by the syllogism composed of the categorical and the conditional, then the opposition (contradiction) obtains between the results of the compositions or between the results and the non-shared term, because what contradicts the necessary consequence contradicts the antecedent. Example of the first: it produces: always either some B is A or some C is D (exclusive disjunction), given 'every C is B' and 'every H is C'. Always either every C is A or every H is D (exclusive disjunction).
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage explains the condition for a valid compound syllogism where one premise is a categorical (حمليه) and the other is a disjunctive exclusive (مانعة الجمع), i.e., a disjunction that excludes the possibility of both parts being true simultaneously but allows both to be false. The author states that for such a syllogism to produce a conclusion, every result (نتيجة) obtained from composing the categorical with each part of the disjunctive (via a separate conditional syllogism) must entail (be productive of) the shared term (الطرف المشارك) that appears in the disjunctive. Then the overall conclusion is a new disjunctive exclusive (مانعة الجمع) formed either from the results of those compositions (if all parts of the disjunctive share the predicate of the categorical) or from those results together with the non-shared term (if only some parts share). The author then justifies this by noting that if the shared term is necessarily implied by the result of the composition (using a mixed categorical-conditional syllogism), then a contradiction (منافاة) arises between the results or between a result and the non-shared term, because what contradicts the necessary consequent (اللازم) contradicts the antecedent (الملزوم). This is a standard principle in hypothetical syllogisms. The example given (though somewhat garbled in OCR) illustrates the first case: given 'every C is B' and 'every H is C', combined with the disjunctive that 'either some B is A or some C is D', the conclusion is a new disjunctive: 'either every C is A or every H is D'. The example demonstrates how the shared terms (B and C) allow the derivation of a new disjunctive conclusion. This type of reasoning is common in Islamic logic when handling mixed compound syllogisms, used in kalām arguments about necessity and contingency.

Key Terms
منفصلة مانعة الجمع
نتيجة التأليف
الطرف المشارك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The categorical must share its subject with all parts of the disjunctive.
B) Every composition result must entail the shared term.
C) The disjunction must be universally quantified.
D) The categorical must be convertible.

Key Takeaway
A mixed categorical-disjunctive syllogism with exclusive disjunction yields a valid conclusion only if each composition's result entails the shared term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Proof and Illustration of the Condition
Proof and example of the condition for mixed syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's proof using the principle that contradicting the necessary consequent contradicts the antecedent, and apply it to a given example.

Core Idea
If the shared term is implied by the result, contradicting it contradicts the source.

After stating the condition, the author provides a logical justification: if the shared term is a necessary consequence of the composition result, then a contradiction between results (or against the non-shared term) implies a contradiction in the original premises. Let's examine the proof and a concrete example.

Guided Path
1
State the proof principle
The author invokes: 'al-munāfī li-l-lāzim yakūnu munāfiyan li-l-malzūm' – what contradicts the necessary consequent contradicts the antecedent.
This is a standard principle in hypothetical syllogisms.
2
Identify the antecedent and consequent
The shared term (al-ṭaraf al-mushārik) is the lazim (necessary consequent) of the composition result. The composition result itself is the malzum (antecedent).
Thus, a contradiction in the composition results leads to a contradiction in the original categorical-disjunctive pair.
3
Apply to the example
In the example, with categoricals 'Every C is B' and 'Every H is C', and disjunction 'Either some B is A or some C is D', the composition results (if they entail the shared term) lead to a new exclusive disjunction 'Either every C is A or every H is D'.
The OCR example is incomplete, but the logical structure is clear.
4
Verify the condition is met
Check if each composition result indeed entails the shared term. In a sound instance, it does, hence the new disjunction follows.

Second Condition for a Valid Disjunctive Syllogism
Author states the second condition for production of a disjunctive syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second condition for a productive disjunctive syllogism and determine what type of conditional it yields.

Core Idea
A disjunctive syllogism yields a particular negative conditional only when the shared terms form a productive composition.

In this lesson, we examine the second condition that must be met for a disjunctive syllogism to produce a valid conclusion. The author specifies that if the shared part (al-taraf al-mushtarak) combined with a shared categorical premise yields a result (natijat al-ta'lif), then a particular negative conditional can be deduced. This condition prevents fallacious inferences and ensures a proper logical connection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared part and the categorical premise
Find the term that appears in both the disjunctive premise and the categorical premise. This is the 'shared part' (al-taraf al-mushtarak).
2
Check if the two shared parts form a productive composition
The shared part together with the categorical premise must be capable of producing a syllogistic conclusion on their own. If they do not form a valid syllogism, the condition fails.
3
Identify the resulting conditional
If the composition is productive, the syllogism yields a particular negative conditional (muttasila juz'iyya saliba). Its antecedent is the result of the composition (natijat al-ta'lif), and its consequent is the other part of the original disjunctive.
The text emphasizes that this conditional does not follow from the mere combination of results of other compositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرط الثاني للإنتاج: و إن كان الطرف المشارك مع الحملية المشاركة منتجاً لنتيجة التأليف - و ذلك إنما يكون إن لو كان المتشاركان على تأليف منتج - فتنتج متصلة جزئية سالبة، مقدمها نتيجة التأليف و تاليها الطرف الآخر، و لا تلزم من نتائج التأليفات.
The second condition for production: And if the shared part together with the shared categorical produces a result of the composition – and that only occurs if the two shared parts are upon a productive composition – then a particular negative conditional is produced, its antecedent being the result of the composition and its consequent the other part, and it does not follow from the results of the compositions.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage states the second condition for a disjunctive syllogism to be productive. If the shared term (al-taraf al-mushtarak) together with the categorical that shares it produces a conclusion (the 'result of the composition'), then the syllogism yields a particular negative conditional (muttasila juz'iyya saliba). The antecedent of this conditional is the result of the composition, and the consequent is the other part of the original disjunctives. Importantly, this conditional does not follow from the mere combination of the results of the compositions; it is a new entailment. The condition requires that the two shared parts (the shared term in the categorical and its counterpart in the conditional) themselves form a productive composition. This ensures the logical connection needed for the conclusion.

Key Terms
متصلة جزئية سالبة
نتيجة التأليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

The Second Condition for Producing a Negative Partial Hypothetical
Author explains the second condition for producing a negative partial hypothetical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a compound syllogism with an affirmative disjunctive premise satisfies the second condition and produce the corresponding negative partial hypothetical.

Core Idea
The second condition governs when a productive composition yields a negative partial hypothetical from an affirmative disjunctive.

Compound syllogisms combining a categorical and a disjunctive premise do not always produce a conclusion. The second condition specifies that if the shared term leads to a productive composition, a negative partial hypothetical is produced. This rule is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology, where disjunctive premises often appear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the categorical premise (e.g., 'Every intoxicating drink is haram') and the affirmative disjunctive premise (e.g., 'Either this drink is intoxicating or this drink is healthy').
The disjunctive must be affirmative for this condition to apply.
2
Find the shared term
Identify the term that appears in both the categorical premise and one of the disjuncts. In the example, 'intoxicating' is the shared term between the categorical and the first disjunct.
The shared term may be a concept, not necessarily the exact same word.
3
Check productive composition
Determine if the shared term forms a productive syllogism with the categorical premise. For instance, from 'Every intoxicating drink is haram' and 'This drink is intoxicating', we deduce 'This drink is haram'. This deduction is the result of composition.
If the categorical gives no new information about the shared term, composition is not productive.
4
Construct the negative partial hypothetical
If composition is productive, the result becomes the antecedent, and the other disjunct becomes the consequent of a negative partial hypothetical. Example: 'If this drink is haram, then it is not healthy.' Ensure that the antecedent does not entail the consequent (otherwise the disjunctive would be false).
The passage states that such production is valid only under this condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشرط الثاني للإنتاج
و إن كان الطرف المشارك مع الحملية المشاركة منتجاً لنتيجة التأليف
- و ذلك إنما يكون إن لو كان المتشاركان على تأليف منتج -
فتنتج متصلة جزئية سالبةً، مقدّمها نتيجة التأليف و تاليها الطرف الآخر،
و لا يلزم من نتائج التأليفات.
برهان إنتاج المتصلة الجزئية السالبة:
أما الأول فلأنه لو استلزمت نتيجة التأليف الطرف الآخر يلزم استلزام الطرف المنتج لذلك الطرف فكذبت المنفصلة.
و لا يلزم مقدمها - بخلاف ما ذكر - الجواز كون نتيجة التأليف أعم من الطرف الآخر.
برهان عدم إنتاج المتصلة الجزئية السالبة بين نتائج التأليفات:
و أما الثاني، فلجواز تلازم لازمي المتعاندين تارة و تعاندها أخرى. و تحقيق ذلك في المواد ظاهر
هذا كله إذا كانت المنفصلة موجبة.
المؤلف من منفصلة سالبة:
أما إذا كانت سالبة، فحكمها:
إذا كانت مانعة الجمع كحكم مانعة الخلو إذا كانت موجبة في الإنتاج و شرائطه.
و بالعكس إلا في إنتاج المتصلة الجزئية.
وجوب كون النتيجة سالبة:
و النتيجة تكون سالبة و إلا كذبت المنفصلة:
أما في مانعة الجمع، فلكون المتشاركين على تأليف منتج - كما في مانعة الخلو حيث كانت موجبة
And the second condition for production: If the term shared with the participating categorical produces a result of the composition - and that only occurs if the two shared terms are on a productive composition - then a negative partial hypothetical is produced, whose antecedent is the result of the composition and whose consequent is the other term. And it does not follow from the results of the compositions. Proof of production of the negative partial hypothetical: As for the first, because if the result of the composition entailed the other term, then the productive term would entail that term, so the disjunctive would be false. And its antecedent does not entail - contrary to what was mentioned - the possibility that the result of the composition is more general than the other term. Proof of non-production of the negative partial hypothetical between the results of compositions: As for the second, because of the possibility that the concomitants of two opposing things sometimes are mutually concomitant and sometimes opposing. And the verification of that in the matters is apparent. All this when the disjunctive is affirmative. Composition from a negative disjunctive: If it is negative, then its rule: If it is exclusive, it is like the rule of non-exclusive when affirmative in production and its conditions. And vice versa, except in the production of the partial hypothetical. Necessity of the conclusion being negative: And the conclusion must be negative, otherwise the disjunctive would be false: In the exclusive, because the two shared terms are on a productive composition - as in the non-exclusive when it is affirmative.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage discusses the second condition for producing certain types of conclusions when a categorical premise is combined with a disjunctive premise. The author explains that if the term shared between the categorical and the disjunctive yields a result (i.e., the two shared terms form a productive syllogism), then a negative partial hypothetical is produced, with the antecedent being that result and the consequent being the other term. However, this does not follow from the results of other compositions. Two proofs are given: for production, if the result entailed the other term it would make the disjunctive false, but the antecedent does not entail it due to possible generality; for non-production, the concomitants of opposing things can sometimes be concomitant and sometimes opposing, so it is not necessary. All this applies to affirmative disjunctives. For negative disjunctives, if it is exclusive, it follows the rule of non-exclusive affirmative; and vice versa, except regarding the production of the partial hypothetical. The conclusion must be negative, otherwise the disjunctive would be false, because in the exclusive case the shared terms are on a productive composition.

Key Terms
الشرط الثاني للإنتاج
متصلة جزئية سالبة
نتيجة التأليف
منفصلة موجبة

Key Takeaway
The second condition allows deduction of a negative partial hypothetical from a compound syllogism with an affirmative disjunctive, provided the composition is productive and the result does not entail the other term.

Rules for Negative Disjunctives in Compound Syllogisms
Author explains the rules for negative disjunctives, including mirroring affirmative rules and requiring a negative conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules for negative disjunctives in compound syllogisms, including the mirroring of affirmative rules and the necessity of a negative conclusion.

Core Idea
Negative disjunctives invert the rules of affirmative ones, and conclusions must be negative to preserve truth.

After discussing affirmative disjunctives, the passage turns to negative disjunctives. Here, the rules are symmetric: an exclusive negative disjunctive behaves like an affirmative non-exclusive, and vice versa, except for the production of the partial hypothetical. Additionally, any conclusion drawn from a negative disjunctive must itself be negative, otherwise the disjunctive would be false. This is crucial for correctly handling negative disjunctions in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of negative disjunctive
Determine whether the negative disjunctive is exclusive (man'at al-jam') or non-exclusive (man'at al-khuluw). In the passage, the example is 'إذا كانت مانعة الجمع' meaning exclusive.
Exclusive means both disjuncts cannot be true together; non-exclusive means both can be true but not both false.
2
Apply the mirror rule
For an exclusive negative disjunctive, treat it like an affirmative non-exclusive disjunctive in production and conditions. For a non-exclusive negative disjunctive, treat it like an affirmative exclusive disjunctive. The passage explicitly states this.
The exception is the production of the partial hypothetical, which follows different rules.
3
Ensure the conclusion is negative
When a negative disjunctive is used, the conclusion of the compound syllogism must be negative. If it were affirmative, the disjunctive would be false because an affirmative conclusion would entail one of the disjuncts.
This necessity is explained: 'والنتيجة تكون سالبة وإلا كذبت المنفصلة'.

Rules for Negative Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author explains rules for disjunctive syllogisms with negative premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion type and productivity conditions for negative disjunctive syllogisms by applying the reversal rule between exclusive and exhaustive disjunctions.

Core Idea
Negative exclusive behaves like affirmative exhaustive; negative exhaustive behaves like affirmative exclusive.

In the previous lesson, we learned about affirmative disjunctive syllogisms. Now, when the disjunctive premise is negative, the rules flip: a negative exclusive disjunction follows the pattern of an affirmative exhaustive, and vice versa. This reversal is key to correctly deriving conclusions in such arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive type and polarity
First, determine whether the disjunction is exclusive (man'at al-jam': both cannot be true) or exhaustive (man'at al-khuluw: both cannot be false), and whether it is affirmative or negative.
For example, 'It is not the case that both A and B are true' could be a negative exclusive.
2
Apply the reversal rule
If the disjunctive is negative exclusive, treat it as if it were an affirmative exhaustive for inference purposes. Conversely, if it is negative exhaustive, treat it as affirmative exclusive.
The author states: 'if it is exclusive, its ruling is like the ruling of exhaustive when it is affirmative.'
3
Set the conclusion polarity
The conclusion of such a syllogism must be negative. Otherwise, the disjunctive premise would be falsified.
Check the reasoning: if the conclusion were affirmative, it would imply both parts are true (for exclusive) or both false (for exhaustive), contradicting the negative disjunction.
4
Note the exception for partial conjunctive
The negative partial conjunctive (al-muttasila al-juz'iyya al-saliba) does not follow from these disjunctive syllogisms.
This is a specific exception mentioned by the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا كله إذا كانت المنفصلة موجبة. أما إذا كانت سالبة، فحكمها: إذا كانت مانعة الجمع فحكمها كحكم «مانعة الخلو إذا كانت موجبة في الإنتاج و شرائطه. و بالعكس إلا في إنتاج المتصلة الجزئية. و النتيجة تكون سالبة و إلا كذبت المنفصلة. أما في مانعة الجمع فلكون المتشاركين على تأليف منتج كما في مانعة الحلو حيث كانت موجبة. وأما في مانعة الخلو فلأن نتيجة التأليف إذا كانت منتجة للطرف المشارك كما في مانعة الجمع حيث كانت موجبة. ولا تلزم المتصلة الجزئية السالبة. القسم الثالث: المؤلف من الحقيقية فتنتج حيث تنتج مانعة الجمع و مانعة الخلق موجبتين، لاستلزامها إياها: بخلاف السالبة فإنّها أعم من سالبقيها، و لازم الأخص لا يجب كونه لازما للأعم.
All this is when the disjunctive is affirmative. But if it is negative, its ruling: if it is exclusive (man'at al-jam'), its ruling is like the ruling of 'exhaustive (man'at al-khuluw) when it is affirmative in production and its conditions. And the opposite, except in the production of the partial conjunctive. And the conclusion must be negative; otherwise the disjunctive is falsified. As for exclusive: because the two participants in a productive composition are like in exclusive of empty when it was affirmative. And as for exhaustive: because the result of the composition if it produces the participating side, as in exclusive when it was affirmative. And the negative partial conjunctive does not follow. The third section: composed of the true [disjunction]. So it produces where exclusive and exhaustive produce when both are affirmative, because it implies them. Unlike the negative, for it is more general than its two negatives, and the necessary of the more specific does not have to be necessary for the more general.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage explains how disjunctive syllogisms behave when the disjunctive premise is negative, and then introduces the 'true disjunction' (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya). The author first states that the previous rules applied to affirmative disjunctives. For negative ones, the rulings are reversed: an exclusive negative (man'at al-jam' saliba) behaves like an exhaustive affirmative (man'at al-khuluw mūjiba) in terms of productivity and conditions, and vice versa, except in the case of producing a partial conjunctive conclusion. The conclusion of such syllogisms must be negative, otherwise the disjunctive premise would be falsified. The reasoning is given: for exclusive, if two participants (i.e., the two parts of the disjunctive) are combined in a productive composition, then if the conclusion is affirmative, it would imply that both parts are true, contradicting the exclusive disjunction. For exhaustive, a similar reasoning applies. Then the author notes that the negative partial conjunctive (al-muttasila al-juz'iyya al-saliba) is not entailed. Finally, the third type is the 'true disjunction' (al-haqiqiyya) which combines both exclusivity and exhaustiveness. It produces a conclusion wherever both exclusive and exhaustive (when affirmative) produce one, because it implies them. But if the true disjunctive is negative, it does not produce as such because it is more general than its two negative components, and the necessary of the more specific is not necessarily necessary for the more general. This is a nuanced discussion of the rules of disjunctive syllogisms, distinguishing between the three types under negation. Students should memorise the conditions: for exclusive negative, behave like exhaustive affirmative; for exhaustive negative, behave like exclusive affirmative; for true disjunctive, it combines both but its negative form is weaker.

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
الحقيقية
المتصلة الجزئية السالبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Conjunctive affirmative

Key Takeaway
A negative exclusive disjunction behaves like an affirmative exhaustive, and a negative exhaustive behaves like an affirmative exclusive; the conclusion must always be negative.

The True Disjunction (al-Munfasila al-Haqiqiyya)
Author explains the true disjunction and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the true disjunction from exclusive and exhaustive, and explain why its negative form does not entail the same conclusions.

Core Idea
The true disjunction implies both exclusive and exhaustive; negatively, it becomes too general to entail the same.

The author introduces a third type of disjunction: the 'true disjunction' (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya), which combines the properties of both exclusive and exhaustive—exactly one part is true. Understanding its logical behavior is crucial because its affirmative form is stronger, but its negative form is weaker and does not entail the same conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of the true disjunction
The true disjunction (al-haqiqiyya) is one where both parts cannot be true together (like exclusive) and both parts cannot be false together (like exhaustive). Exactly one disjunct is true.
It is the standard logical 'exclusive or' (XOR) in modern terms.
2
Recognize the implication relationship
An affirmative true disjunction implies both an affirmative exclusive disjunction and an affirmative exhaustive disjunction.
The author says: 'it produces where exclusive and exhaustive produce when both are affirmative, because it implies them.'
3
Analyze the negative true disjunction
When the true disjunction is negative, it does not imply negative exclusive or negative exhaustive. The reason: the negative true disjunction is more general than each of its negative components, and what is necessary for the more specific is not necessarily necessary for the more general.
For example, ~(A xor B) is equivalent to (A & B) v (~A & ~B), which is wider in scope.
4
Apply in practice
Given an affirmative true disjunction, you can derive the same conclusions as from its component exclusive and exhaustive disjunctions. But if it is negative, you cannot directly infer those conclusions.
This distinction is important when dealing with real arguments in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Inference Rules for Exclusive and Exhaustive Disjunctions
Author explains inference rules for māni'at al-jam' and māni'at al-khuluw

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the inference rules for māni'at al-jam' and māni'at al-khuluw to determine the truth of a disjunctive proposition in an Islamic legal syllogism.

Core Idea
Impossibility with the necessary implies impossibility with the implicant; same for separation.

This passage details how to reason with two fundamental types of disjunctive propositions in logic: exclusive (māni'at al-jam') and exhaustive (māni'at al-khuluw). Understanding these rules is essential for evaluating legal arguments in usul al-fiqh, where a mujtahid often confronts mutually exclusive or exhaustive options. The passage also touches on compound disjunctions from actual premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction type
Read the disjunctive premise carefully. Is it exclusive (māni'at al-jam': both cannot be true together) or exhaustive (māni'at al-khuluw: at least one must be true)?
For example, 'The contract is either valid or void' is exclusive; 'The debtor pays now or later' is exhaustive.
2
Extract the terms and the shared implication
Label the two disjuncts as A and B. Identify the derived conclusion C that shares a term with one disjunct (e.g., C implies A).
In the text, 'the sharing term necessitates the result' means that one disjunct implies C.
3
Apply the rule for exclusivity or exhaustivity
For exclusive: if C and B cannot be true together, then A and B cannot be true together. This confirms the impossibility of combination, and the disjunction is false. For exhaustive: if C and B cannot both be false (i.e., at least one true), then A and B cannot both be false, confirming the disjunction is true.
The text states: 'impossibility of co-occurrence with the necessary necessitates impossibility with the implicant'.
4
Conclude about the original disjunction
Based on the inference, determine whether the original disjunctive claim holds. If the impossibility extends, the disjunction is shown to be false (for exclusive) or true (for exhaustive).
Note that for the compound (haqīqiyya) type, the negative does not produce the same result.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ب يلزم استلزام الطرف المشارك لنتيجة التأليف. فلو امتنع الجمع بين نتيجة التأليف و بين الطرف الآخر إن كان غير مشارك، أو بين نتيجته إن كان مشاركاً، د يلزم امتناع الجمع بين الطرفين لأن امتناع الاجتماع مع اللازم يوجب امتناعه مع الملزوم، ه فكذبت المنفصلة. و أما في مانعة الخلو فلأن نتيجة التأليف إذا كانت منتجة للطرف المشارك كما في مانعة الجمع حيث كانت موجبة. ب فلو امتنع الخلق بين نتيجة التأليف و الطرف الآخر أو بين نتيجته، يلزم امتناع الخلق بين الطرفين لأن امتناع الخلق مع الملزوم يوجب امتناعه مع اللازم. د ولا تلزم المتصلة الجزئية السالبة الحقيقية الموجبة: الجواز استلزام لازم أحد الكاذبين للآخر. القسم الثالث: المؤلف من الحقيقية. فتنتج حيث تنتج مانعة الجمع و مانعة الخلق موجبتين ب لاستلزامها إياها بخلاف السالبة أ فإنّها أعم من سالبقيها، و لازم الأخص لا يجب كونه لازما للأعم.
And as for [the case where] the sharing term necessitates the result of the composition. So if combination is impossible between the result of the composition and the other term if it is non-sharing, or between its result if it is sharing, then it necessitates impossibility of combination between the two terms, because impossibility of co-occurrence with the necessary (lāzim) necessitates impossibility with its implicant (malzūm); thus the disjunction is false. And as for the exhaustive disjunction (māni'at al-khuluw), because if the result of the composition is productive for the sharing term as in the exclusive disjunction where it was affirmative, so if separation is impossible between the result of the composition and the other term, or between its result, then it necessitates impossibility of separation between the two terms, because impossibility of separation with the implicant necessitates impossibility with the necessary. And the particular negative conditional proposition does not entail the affirmative 'actual' (haqīqiyya) [proposition]: the permissibility of the necessary of one of the two falsehoods implying the other. The third section: the compound from the actual [premises]. So it produces where the exclusive and exhaustive disjunctions produce, both affirmative, because it necessitates them, unlike the negative, for it is more general than its two negatives, and the more specific necessary does not have to be necessary for the more general.
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage explains the inference rules for two types of disjunctive syllogisms: exclusive disjunction (māni'at al-jam') and exhaustive disjunction (māni'at al-khuluw). The author first describes the exclusive case: if the composition (the syllogism) yields a conclusion that shares a term with the disjunctive premise, and if the combination of that conclusion with the other term of the disjunction is impossible, then the impossibility extends to the combination of the two original terms of the disjunction, making the disjunction false. This is because impossibility with a necessary implication (lāzim) entails impossibility with its implicant (malzūm). Then for the exhaustive case, similar reasoning applies but with separation (khuluw) instead of combination. The author also notes that a particular negative conditional (muttaṣila juz'iyya sāliba) does not entail an affirmative 'actual' proposition, because it allows two falsehoods where one's necessary might imply the other. Finally, the third type (compound from actual premises) produces results similar to the two types when affirmative, but not when negative, because the negative is more general and the specific necessary does not apply to the general.

The Syllogism
