Exchange of Rulings Between Disjunctions
Author explains the exchange of rulings between disjunctions via negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to transform an exclusive disjunction into an exhaustive disjunction by negating its parts and verify the equivalence.

Core Idea
Exclusive and exhaustive disjunctions exchange rulings by negating their parts.

In Islamic logic, disjunctive propositions come in two types: exclusive (māni'at al-jam') and exhaustive (māni'at al-khuluw). Understanding how to convert one into the other is crucial for analyzing legal and theological arguments. This lesson will show you the exchange rule using negation of the parts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disjunction
Determine whether the given disjunction is exclusive (both parts cannot be true together) or exhaustive (both parts cannot be false together). Look for clues like 'either...or...' and context.
Exclusive: لا يجتمعان (they do not combine); Exhaustive: لا يخلوان (they do not both fail).
2
Negate each disjunct
Take the contradictory opposite (naqīḍ) of each part. If the original disjunct is a positive term, its negation is the negative; if it's a negative, its negation is the positive.
Ensure the negation is the exact contradictory, not just contrary.
3
Form the new disjunction of the negations
Combine the two negated parts into a new disjunction. If the original was exclusive, the new one is exhaustive, and vice versa.
The exchange works because the truth conditions swap: if neither A nor B can be true together, then not-A and not-B cannot be false together.
4
Verify the equivalence
Check that the original disjunction and the new disjunction are logically equivalent: they have the same truth value under all assignments.
This is due to the principle of double negation and De Morgan's laws.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبادل الأحكام بين مانعة الجمع و مانعة الخلق بتبادل نقائض الأجزاء وكل واحدة من المنفصلتين، موجبة و سالبة: تنتج حيث تنتج صاحبتها إذا كان أجزاؤها نقائض أجزاء صاحبتها، لارتدادها إلى صاحبتها. تنبيه: قياسات من الحملية المردّدة المحمول إذا كان موضوع أجزاء الانفصال واحداً و مورد التقسيم «كل واحد واحد» لا الكل. تنتج مع الحملية: في الأول و الثالث و الوسط موضوع الأجزاء، و في الأول و الثاني و الوسط محمولات الأجزاء جميعاً. و تكون النتيجة كالكبرى في الجنس و الكيف. لكن القياس يكون أشبه بالقياس الحملة. مثال الأول: ينتج: كل ج ب و كل ب (إما د و إما ه).. «كل ج (إما د و إما ه)
Exchange of rulings between the exclusive disjunction and the exhaustive disjunction by exchanging negations of the parts. And each one of the two disjunctions, affirmative and negative: it produces where its counterpart produces if its parts are the negations of the parts of its counterpart, because it reduces to its counterpart. A note: Syllogisms from categorical with repeated predicate if the subject of the parts of the disjunction is one and the division applies to 'each one individually', not the whole. It produces with the categorical: in the first and third and middle [figures], the subject of the parts, and in the first and second and middle [figures], the predicates of all the parts. And the conclusion is like the major in genus and quality. But the syllogism is more similar to the categorical syllogism. The first example: It produces: Every J is B, and every B is (either D or H), therefore every J is (either D or H).
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage covers two points. First, the exchange of rulings between the two types of disjunctions: an exclusive disjunction (māni'at al-jam') can be transformed into an exhaustive disjunction (māni'at al-khuluw) by negating its parts, and vice versa. This works because the negated disjunction reduces to its counterpart. Second, a note (tanbīh) on syllogisms composed of a categorical premise and a disjunctive premise with a disjunctive predicate (al-ḥamliyya al-muraddad al-mahmūl). Here the subject of the disjunctive parts must be the same, and the division applies to each individual, not the whole. The syllogism produces a conclusion where the subject is from the first and third and middle (figures) and the predicates are from the first and second and middle. The conclusion resembles the major premise in genus and quality, but the syllogism resembles a categorical syllogism more closely. An example is given: Every J is B, and every B is either D or H, therefore every J is either D or H. This is a valid syllogism in the first figure (a categorical with a disjunctive predicate).

Mixed Syllogisms with Disjunctive Predicate
Author discusses mixed syllogisms with disjunctive predicates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct valid first-figure syllogisms where one premise is categorical and the other has a disjunctive predicate.

Core Idea
A categorical with disjunctive predicate combined with another categorical yields a disjunctive conclusion in the first figure.

Some syllogisms mix categorical and disjunctive premises. This lesson focuses on the case where the major premise is a categorical proposition with a disjunctive predicate (al-ḥamliyya al-muraddad al-mahmūl). You will learn how such syllogisms work and how to evaluate their validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the two premises: one categorical (e.g., 'Every J is B') and another with a disjunctive predicate (e.g., 'Every B is either D or H'). The subject of the disjunctive parts must be the same individual term.
The disjunctive predicate applies to each individual, not the whole class collectively.
2
Check the figure
Determine the figure by identifying the middle term (B). In the example, B is both the predicate of the first premise and the subject of the second: this is the first figure (middle term subject in minor, predicate in major).
The author states it works in first, third, and middle figures for the subject of the parts.
3
Form the conclusion
The conclusion takes the subject from the minor premise (J) and the disjunctive predicate from the major (either D or H). The conclusion is a categorical with disjunctive predicate: 'Every J is either D or H'.
The conclusion resembles the major premise in genus and quality.
4
Verify validity
Since the middle term distributes correctly in the first figure (B is universal in major and minor), and the disjunctive predicate is universal, the syllogism is valid. The conclusion follows necessarily.
This mixed syllogism is essentially a categorical syllogism with a complex predicate.

Condition for Production in Disjunctive Syllogisms and Subject Restriction
Author discusses condition for production and subject restriction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition of 'truth of prevention of emptiness' for a disjunctive premise and explain why the subject must be restricted to 'each one' to avoid reduction.

Core Idea
A disjunctive premise must prevent emptiness to produce a conclusion, and the subject must be distributive.

In this passage, the author explains two key conditions for valid disjunctive syllogisms: the disjunctive premise must satisfy the condition of 'prevention of emptiness' (man' al-khuluw), and the subject of the disjunction must be expressed distributively as 'each one' rather than collectively. These conditions ensure that the conclusion necessarily follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise type
The text mentions 'preventing-union' vs 'preventing-emptiness'. Note that the condition of truth of prevention of emptiness applies particularly to 'preventing-union' because if both parts can be false, the syllogism fails to produce a conclusion.
2
Apply the condition of truth of prevention of emptiness
Check that the disjunctive premise is such that it cannot be that all its parts are false. This is required for the syllogism to produce a conclusion.
This condition is called shart al-intaj.
3
Understand the restriction with 'each one'
The subject of the disjunction must be restricted to 'kull wahid wahid' (each one) because using 'all' would collapse the syllogism into simpler forms already covered. The author gives an example: 'Every J is B' and 'Either every B is D or every B is H' – this is actually a compound that reduces.
The distributive reading ensures the disjunction applies to individuals, not the whole class collectively.
4
Reason why the restriction is needed
Without the distributive reading, the disjunction would apply to the whole class as a unity, making it a universal categorical proposition, which changes the syllogistic form and makes it reducible to earlier cases.

The first method: that the middle be a complete part of the two premises, and consideration of the sharing of the antecedent and consequent of the conjunctive because the antecedent of the disjunctive is not distinguished from its consequent. Then: A) If the conjunctive is minor, it does not distinguish the first figure from the second, nor the third from the fourth. B) And if it is major, it does not distinguish the first from the third, nor the second from the fourth. And if that is the case, the division of this method is not by consideration of figures, but rather the conjunctive is minor or major.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of syllogisms involving disjunctive and conjunctive premises, typical of advanced logic treatises. It begins with an example: 'Every number is either odd or even' and 'Everything that is odd or even is composed of units' which yields 'Every number is composed of units.' This illustrates a disjunctive syllogism. The author then states the condition for production (shart al-intaj): the truth of 'prevention of emptiness' (man' al-khuluw) on the disjunctive premise. This means that in a disjunctive of the 'preventing-union' type, if both parts can be false, the conclusion does not follow. Next, the author discusses a restriction: the subject of the disjunction must be expressed as 'each one' (kull wahid wahid), not 'all' (al-kull). For example, if we say 'Every J is B' and 'Either every B is D or every B is H', this is actually a compound of a categorical and a disjunction, but it reduces to simpler forms if 'all' is used. Then, reference is made to Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) in al-Shifa, who required the disjunctive in the minor premise to be positive when it shares parts with the categoricals in the major. However, the author notes a consideration ('fihi nazar'): whether the disjunctive is positive or negative, as long as its parts are positive, the syllogism produces a conclusion under the stated conditions. Finally, the passage introduces the fifth section on compound syllogisms from conjunctive and disjunctive premises, outlining the first method. Here the middle term must be a complete part of both premises. Because the antecedent and consequent of the conjunctive are not distinguished from the disjunctive's parts, the figure of the syllogism becomes ambiguous: if the conjunctive is minor, figures 1 and 2 are not distinguished; if major, figures 1 and 3 are not distinguished. Therefore, for this method, the division is not by figures but by whether the conjunctive is minor or major.

D) Negativity of the disjunctive

Key Takeaway
A disjunctive premise must be such that it cannot be that all its parts are false (prevention of emptiness), and its subject must be understood distributively to avoid reduction to simpler syllogistic forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following argument: 'Every action is either obligatory or permissible; everything obligatory or permissible is legally ruled; therefore every action is legally ruled.' Check whether the disjunctive premise satisfies the condition of 'prevention of emptiness' and whether the subject is correctly restricted to 'each one'.
